First Response to Objection: Showing the Judgment Without the Opponent's Description
Author explains the first method of answering an objection by showing the judgment stands without the opponent's proposed description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the first method of responding to a mu'aarada in Islamic dialectic: presenting a case where the judgment holds without the opponent's proposed description.

Core Idea
First response to a causal objection: show the ruling exists where the opponent's description is absent.

In dialectical argument, when an opponent challenges your claimed cause by proposing an alternative description, you can refute him directly. The first method is to find a specific instance where the disputed ruling applies, yet the opponent's description is missing. This proves that the opponent's description is not the necessary cause, and your cause stands alone. The passage illustrates this with the classic debate between al-Shafi'i and the Hanafis on the cause of riba in wheat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claimed 'illa
In the example, al-Shafi'i claims the cause of riba in wheat is sustenance (t'am). Locate this in the passage.
The 'illa is the ratio legis that justifies the ruling.
2
Identify the opponent's alternative description
The Hanafi objector proposes measurement (kayl) as the real cause. Find where this is introduced in the passage.
The mu'aarada challenges the proponent's 'illa by offering a competing description.
3
Find a case where the ruling applies but the opponent's description is absent
Al-Shafi'i points to a small handful of wheat: it is not measured due to small size, yet the prohibition of riba applies. This counterexample shows the ruling exists without measurement.
The case must be a real, accepted instance of the ruling.
4
Conclude that the opponent's description is not the cause
Since the ruling is present without measurement, measurement cannot be the necessary condition. Therefore, the objector's proposed cause is invalid, and the proponent's original 'illa stands.
This method does not prove the proponent's cause is the only cause, only that the opponent's is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن للمستدل أن يجيب عن المعارضة بأجوبة متعددة، فيجيب عن المعارضة في الأصل بعدة طرق : الأولى : أن يبين المستدل أن مثل الحكم المتنازع فيه ثبت بدون الوصف الذي أبداه المعترض، فيستقل بالعلية ما ذكره المستدل . ومثاله قول الشافعي علة تحريم الربا في البر الطعم، فيعارضه الحنفي مثلاً بوصف الكيل، فيقول الشافعي : إن ملء الكف ينتفي عنه الكيل لقلته ، ومنع الربا موجود فيه، فيستقل الطعم بالعلية. والقصد المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال.
Know that the proponent can answer the objection with multiple responses, so he responds to the objection regarding the original case in several ways: The first: that the proponent shows that the disputed judgment was established without the description which the objector proposed, so the cause mentioned by the proponent stands alone. Its example is the statement of al-Shafi'i: the cause of prohibition of riba in wheat is sustenance, so the Hanafi objects, for example, with the description of measurement, so al-Shafi'i says: a handful is exempt from measurement due to its smallness, yet the prohibition of riba exists in it, so sustenance stands alone as the cause. The purpose is the example, not a critique of the statements' evidence.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical explanation of how a proponent (mustadill) can refute an opponent's objection (mu'aarada). The author presents the first of several methods. The method is to demonstrate that the disputed legal ruling (hukm) is present in a case that lacks the description put forward by the objector. This shows that the objector's description is not the real cause ('illa). The example given is from the debate on riba in wheat (al-burr). Al-Shafi'i holds that the cause is 'sustenance' (ta'm). The Hanafi objects by suggesting that the cause is 'measurement' (kayl). Al-Shafi'i responds by pointing to a small handful of wheat: it is not measured (due to small quantity), yet the ruling of riba still applies. Therefore, the objector's proposed description (measurement) is not the cause, and the original cause (sustenance) stands alone. The author notes that the purpose of this example is merely illustrative, not to actually debate the evidence.

Key Terms
المستدل
المعارضة
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage, what does al-Shafi'i's reference to a small handful of wheat demonstrate?
A) That small quantities are not measured.
B) That the ruling of riba exists without measurement.
C) That al-Shafi'i agrees with the Hanafi.
D) That sustenance is not the cause.

Key Takeaway
The first method of answering a causal objection is to present a specific instance where the disputed judgment holds without the description proposed by the opponent, thereby validating one's own claimed cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the debate on the prohibition of khamr (wine). A proponent says the cause is intoxication (sukr). An opponent objects that the cause is liquid state (sayl). Use the first method presented in this lesson to find a counterexample: a liquid that causes intoxication but is not wine? Or better: a solid intoxicant (e.g., hashish) is not a liquid but still prohibited, showing that 'liquid' is not the cause. Construct a similar argument.
?
In a kalam debate, a mutakallim claims the world is created because it is contingent (mumkin). The opponent objects that the cause is being composite (murakkab). Apply the first method: find a case of something contingent but not composite (e.g., a simple substance like an atom) that is still created. This shows composition is not the cause, and contingency stands alone.

Showing Description Is Irrelevant to the Genre of the Ruling
Author explains the second method: the proponent demonstrates that the objector's description is irrelevant to the general category of the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the dialectical method of showing that a description is irrelevant (mulghā) to the genus (jins) of a disputed ruling.

Core Idea
A description that does not affect the genus of the ruling is extraneous to the analogy.

In this passage, the author explains the second way a proponent can respond to an objector's challenge in a dialectical exchange. The proponent must show that the description the objector relies on does not actually influence the general category (jins) of the ruling in question. This method, known as إلغاء الوصف في جنس الحكم, is a powerful tool for defending analogical extensions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original ruling and the proposed analogical extension
Find the ruling at issue (e.g., transfer of emancipation from a hadith about a male slave) and the subcase the proponent wants to include (e.g., a female slave).
2
Locate the objector's description that allegedly prevents the analogy
Look for the attribute the objector claims is unique to the original case (e.g., masculinity) and why it matters (e.g., added legal duties).
The objector argues that this attribute is a 'manā' (preventive description).
3
Examine whether that description has any effect on rulings of that genus
Determine if the description actually influences the type of ruling (e.g., emancipation) in general. If not, it is irrelevant (mulghā).
The proponent asserts that masculinity/femininity are 'tardī' (coincidental) regarding emancipation rulings.
4
Confirm that the description is 'tardī' and thus irrelevant
Check if the description is known in the law to have no effect on that genre of rulings. If so, the objection is answered.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : أن يبين المستدل إلغاء ما ذكره المعترض في جنس الحكم المختلف فيه كالذكورة والأنوثة بالنسبة إلى العتق، ففي الحديث الصحيح من أعتق شِرْكًا له في عبد الحديث، فيقول المستدل : الأمة كالعبد في سراية العتق الواردة في الحديث بجامع الرق. فيقول المعترض: إن في الأصل وصفًا مانعا من إلحاق الفرع به وهو الذكورة؛ لأن عتق الذكر تلزمه مصالح - كالشهادة والجهاد وجميع المناصب المختصة بالرجال - لا توجد في الفرع الذي هو الأمة . فيجيب المستدل عن هذا الاعتراض بأن الذكورة والأنوثة بالنسبة إلى العتق وصفان طرديان لا يترتب عليهما شيء من أحكام العتق، كما هو معلوم في الشرع.
The second: that the proponent shows the cancellation of what the objector mentioned in the genus of the disputed ruling, like masculinity and femininity with respect to emancipation, for in the authentic hadith: 'He who emancipates a share (shirk) in a slave', the proponent says: the female slave is like the male slave regarding the transfer of emancipation mentioned in the hadith due to the commonality of slavery. The objector says: in the original case there is a description preventing the analogical extension of the subcase to it, namely masculinity, because the emancipation of a male entails interests like testimony, jihad, and all positions specific to men, which are not present in the subcase which is the female slave. So the proponent answers this objection by saying that masculinity and femininity with respect to emancipation are two coincidental descriptions (tardiyyan) upon which nothing of the rulings of emancipation follows, as is known in the law.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the second dialectical method for answering an objection. Here the proponent argues that the description proposed by the objector is irrelevant (mulgha) to the genre (jins) of the disputed ruling. The example concerns the legal rule of 'sirāyat al-‘itq' (the transfer of emancipation) when someone frees a share of a slave. The proponent (a Shafi'i or Hanafi? The text does not specify, but likely a Hanafi) analogically extends the hadith (which mentions a male slave) to a female slave, based on the commonality of slavery (al-riqq). The objector objects that the original case (the male slave) has an attribute that prevents the analogy: masculinity, because men have additional legal capacities (like jihad and testimony) that women lack. The proponent counter-argues that masculinity and femininity are 'tardiyya' — coincidental attributes that do not affect the rulings of emancipation at all. Therefore, the objection fails because the proposed description is extraneous to the legal cause.

Key Terms
طردي
الرق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the objector mention masculinity (ذكورة) in this example?
A) To show females cannot own slaves.
B) To argue that the male slave has extra legal duties that affect emancipation.
C) To prove that females are not included in the hadith.
D) To demonstrate that masculinity is a coincidental description.

Key Takeaway
The proponent defeats the objection by showing that the description is irrelevant to the genus of the ruling, preserving the analogy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Consider the analogy between a divorce that is revocable (rajʿī) and one that is irrevocable (bāʾin). If an objector claims that the presence of children in the revocable case prevents analogizing to the irrevocable case, how would you apply the method of showing the description is irrelevant to the genus? Formulate the proponent's response.
?
Task 2: In the provided passage, the objector uses masculinity as a preventing description. Find another example in fiqh where a description like 'gender' or 'age' might be argued as tardī for a genre of rulings, and explain why it fails.

Winning by Text: Using Nass and Ima' in Jadal
Author explains the third method: the proponent demonstrates that his proposed cause is supported by explicit text (nass) or legal allusion (ima').

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a proponent in jadal defeats an objection by citing nass or ima' to establish his cause, and apply this method to a juristic example.

Core Idea
Text and allusion in jadal: if your cause is in the source, alternative descriptions become irrelevant.

When an opponent objects to your claimed cause by offering a different description, you can silence him by proving that your cause is grounded in an explicit text or a clear legal allusion. This lesson unpacks the third response in munazara with two classic examples from riba debates, showing how al-Shafi'i and a Hanbali jurist used this method. You will learn to distinguish nass from ima' and apply them to real juristic arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the third method
The passage says the proponent 'shows that the cause which his opponent objected to is established by a text or an allusion.' This is the third way to refute an objection. Note that the method relies on authority of revelation, not mere reasoning.
The cause in question is the one that the proponent originally claimed, not the opponent's alternative.
2
Distinguish nass from ima'
Nass is an explicit textual statement (e.g., 'the cause is X'). Ima' (allusion) is an indirect indication, often through the structure of a hadith, where the ruling is tied to a specific description. Both are authoritative, but ima' requires inference.
The Hanbali example is described as 'like a text' (ka al-nass), meaning the allusion is so clear it amounts to a text.
3
Analyze the ima' example
Al-Shafi'i claimed the cause of riba is 'sustenance' (ta'm). The Maliki opposed with 'nourishing and storing' (iqtitāt wa-idikhār). Al-Shafi'i responded that sustenance is established by ima' from the hadith 'food for food, like for like.' The Prophet's ordering of stipulating equivalence upon the description 'food' alludes that 'being food' is the cause.
The grammatical connection (ta'līq) between the ruling (equivalence) and the description (food) is key to the ima' method.
4
Analyze the nass example
The Hanbali claimed the cause is 'measurement' (kayl). The Maliki objected with 'nourishing and storing.' The Hanbali replied that measurement is established by a text (a hadith from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri that implies measurement). Although the passage cuts off, the principle is clear: a nass directly supports the cause.
The author emphasizes the examples are illustrative, not a full critique of the positions.
5
Apply the method to a new case
Imagine a debate about the prohibition of khamr. You claim the cause is intoxication (iskār). Your opponent says it is grape juice origin. Show that a hadith like 'Every intoxicant is khamr' uses the description 'intoxicant' in a way that alludes to cause (ima'). Or if there is an explicit verse saying 'intoxicants are evil', that would be nass.
The strength of the response depends on how directly the text or allusion supports the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالثة : أن يبين أن العلة التي عارضها خصمه ثابتةٌ بنص أو إيماء . ومثاله في الإيماء والتنبيه قول الشافعي: العلة في تحريم الربا الطعم، فيعارضه المالكي بالاقتيات والادخار، فيقول الشافعي: إن كون الطعم هو العلة ثبت بمسلك الإيماء والتنبيه في حديث معمر بن عبدالله في صحيح مسلم : كنت أسمع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : (الطعام بالطعام مثلاً بمثل فترتيب اشتراط المثلية على وصف الطعم يدل بمسلك الإيماء والتنبيه على أن العلة الطعم. والقصد مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال . ومثاله فيما هو كالنص أن يقول الحنبلي مثلاً : علة تحريم الربا في البر الكيل فيعارضه المالكي بوصف الاقتيات والادخار، فيقول الحنبلي: إن كون العلة الكيل ثبت مقتضاه بالنص، ففي حديث حيان بن عبيد الله عند الحاكم عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه
The third: that he show that the cause which his opponent objected to is established by a text or an allusion. Its example regarding allusion and indication: the statement of al-Shafi'i: the cause of prohibition of riba is sustenance, so the Maliki opposes with (the description of) nourishing and storing, so al-Shafi'i says: that sustenance is the cause is established by the method of allusion in the hadith of Ma'mar bin Abdullah in Sahih Muslim: 'I heard the Messenger of Allah say: Food for food, like for like', so the sequential order of stipulating equivalence upon the description of sustenance indicates by the method of allusion that the cause is sustenance. The purpose is the absolute example, not a critique of the statements' evidence. Another example which is like a text: that the Hanbali says, for instance: the cause of riba in wheat is measurement, so the Maliki opposes with the description of nourishing and storing, so the Hanbali says: that the cause is measurement is established by its implication from the text, for in the hadith of Hayyan bin Ubaidullah reported by al-Hakim from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may Allah be pleased with him)...
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents the third method of answering an objection: the proponent demonstrates that his claimed cause is supported by either an explicit text (nass) or an allusion (ima') in the primary sources. Two examples are given. The first illustrates allusion (ima'): al-Shafi'i argues that the cause for riba is sustenance, using the method of isharat al-nass (indication of the text) from the hadith where the Prophet (s) says 'food for food, like for like'. The grammatical structure of the hadith links the ruling (equivalence) to the description (food), thereby alluding to it being the cause. The second example is 'like a text': the Hanbali holds that the cause is measurement, based on a hadith that implies measurement, though the passage cuts off. In both cases, when the cause is established from a revealed source, the opponent's alternative description (e.g., nourishing/storing) becomes irrelevant. The author emphasizes that the examples are purely illustrative.

Key Terms
النص
الإيماء والتنبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example, how does al-Shafi'i prove that 'sustenance' is the cause of riba?
A) He quotes a text explicitly stating 'the cause is sustenance.'
B) He uses the method of allusion from the hadith's structure.
C) He argues that all jurists agree on it.
D) He claims that the Maliki has no evidence.

Key Takeaway
When your cause is grounded in a text or clear allusion, it overrides any alternative description proposed by the opponent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a hadith that uses the method of ima' to indicate a cause (e.g., 'Do not sell dates for dates except like for like'), and explain how the wording alludes to the cause.
?
In a debate scenario: your opponent says the cause for the prohibition of wine is 'grape origin'. You claim it is 'intoxication'. Show how you would use the third method (nass or ima') to defend your cause, citing a relevant text from the Quran or Hadith.

Third Tanbih: Proving Your Cause by Text or Implication
Author explains and illustrates proving the cause via text or implication in dialectic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the two methods (nass and ima') for grounding your 'illa in dialectical debate, using Islamic legal examples.

Core Idea
Ground your cause in revelation – text or implication – to trump your opponent's counter-description.

This lesson examines a key dialectical move: when your opponent disputes your cause by offering a competing description, you can answer by showing that your cause is explicitly stated in a text (nass) or indirectly implied by it (ima'). The author illustrates with two examples from riba debates between madhhabs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputants and the contested cause
In each example, note who is making the claim (e.g., al-Shafi'i or the Hanbali) and what cause they propose (food or weight). Identify the opponent (the Maliki) and their counter-description (staple/storable).
2
Locate the opponent's counter-description
The Maliki objects by offering a different description (being staple and storable) as the cause. This is the standard dialectical move of contesting the 'illa.
3
Determine whether the response uses nass or ima'
Check if the response cites an explicit text (nass) that directly states the cause, or an indirect implication (ima') where the cause is inferred from a condition attached to a description.
In the first example, al-Shafi'i uses ima' from the Prophet's order 'Food for food, like for like'. In the second, the Hanbali uses nass from the hadith 'and likewise whatever is weighed or measured'.
4
Explain why that response defeats the objection
By grounding the cause in revelation, the claimant shows that their cause is divinely indicated, making it superior to a mere rational description. The opponent's counter-description is thus irrelevant unless they can also produce textual evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتنبيه الثالثة : أن يبين أن العلة التي عارضها خصمه ثابتةٌ بنص أو إيماء ومثاله في الإيماء والتنبيه قول الشافعي: العلة في تحريم الربا الطعم، فيعارضه المالكي بالاقتيات والادخار، فيقول الشافعي: إن كون الطعم هو العلة ثبت بمسلك الإيماء والتنبيه في حديث معمر بن عبدالله في صحيح مسلم : كنت أسمع رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم يقول : (الطعام بالطعام مثلاً بمثل فترتيب اشتراط المثلية على وصف الطعم يدل بمسلك الإيماء والتنبيه على أن العلة الطعم. والقصد مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال . ومثاله فيما هو كالنص أن يقول الحنبلي مثلاً : علة تحريم الربا في البر الكيل فيعارضه المالكي بوصف الاقتيات والادخار، فيقول الحنبلي: إن كون العلة الكيل ثبت مقتضاه بالنص، ففي حديث حيان بن عبيد الله عند الحاكم عن أبي سعيد الخدري رضي الله عنه بعد أن ذكر الستة المنصوص على تحريم الربا فيها أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : وكذلك كل ما يكال أو يوزن»، وقد بينا في كتابنا أضواء البيان أن هذا الحديث ثابت، وناقشنا من ضعفه، وهو كالنص الواضح على أن معرفة القدر بالكيل والوزن هي علة الربا، وفي الصحيحين بعد ذكر الربويات أن النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم قال : ( وكذلك الميزان)، بعد ذكر الكيل في الحديث .
And the third notification: That he shows that the cause which his opponent opposed is established by text or implication. And its example in implication and notification: The saying of al-Shafi'i: The cause in the prohibition of riba is food (tam). The Maliki opposes him with (the description of) being staple and storable. So al-Shafi'i says: That food is the cause is established by the method of implication and notification in the hadith of Ma'mar ibn Abdullah in Sahih Muslim: I heard the Messenger of God (peace be upon him) say: 'Food for food, like for like.' So the ordering of the condition of equality upon the description of food indicates, by the method of implication and notification, that the cause is food. And the intent is absolute example, not discussion of the proofs of the schools. And its example in what is like text: That the Hanbali, for example, says: The cause of the prohibition of riba in wheat is weight. The Maliki opposes him with the description of being staple and storable. So the Hanbali says: That the cause is weight established by its requirement from text. For in the hadith of Hayyan ibn Ubayd Allah, recorded by al-Hakim from Abu Sa'id al-Khudri (may God be pleased with him) after mentioning the six items specified in the prohibition of riba, that the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'And likewise everything that is weighed or measured.' And we have shown in our book Adwa' al-Bayan that this hadith is authentic, and we have discussed those who weaken it, and it is like clear text that knowledge of quantity by measurement and weight is the cause of riba. And in the two Sahihs after mentioning the ribawi items, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said: 'And likewise the scales,' after mentioning measurement in the hadith.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage presents the third method (tanbih) for answering an objection in dialectical argument: proving that one's own claimed cause ('illa) is established either by an explicit text (nass) or by implication (ima') in the revealed sources. The author gives two examples from Islamic legal reasoning on riba (usury). The first example involves al-Shafi'i, who says the cause of riba in food items is the property of being food (tam). His opponent, the Maliki, counters with the description of being staple and storable. Al-Shafi'i responds by pointing to a hadith in which the Prophet equates food with food in the same quantity; the very condition of equality being tied to 'food' indicates that food is the cause—this is the 'ima' or tanbih method. The author notes that this is merely an illustration, not a full debate of the schools. The second example involves a Hanbali who claims the cause for riba in wheat is weight. The Maliki again objects with the staple/storable description. The Hanbali then cites a hadith that explicitly adds "whatever is measured or weighed" to the list of ribawi items, making weight a text-based (nass) cause. The author references his own book Adwa' al-Bayan to affirm the hadith's authenticity. Both examples demonstrate how a disputant can defend his cause by grounding it in revealed evidence, thereby defusing the opponent's counter-description. The core principle is that if your cause is textually or impliedly supported, it takes precedence.

Key Terms
علة
نص
إيماء
تنبيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the second example, what method does the Hanbali use to prove that weight is the cause?
A) Implication (ima') from the hadith 'Food for food, like for like'.
B) Explicit text (nass) from the hadith 'and likewise everything that is weighed or measured'.
C) Consensus of the scholars.
D) Analogical reasoning from other commodities.

Key Takeaway
When your opponent contests your cause, you can defend it by showing it is supported either by an explicit text (nass) or by an implication (ima') from revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a hypothetical debate, you claim that the cause for riba in rice is weight. The opponent objects that the cause is being staple. Using the hadith 'and likewise everything that is weighed or measured,' show how you would prove your cause by text (nass).
?
Task 2: Identify which method (nass or ima') is used in the first example of the passage, and explain how the hadith 'Food for food, like for like' implies that food is the cause.

beginner
8 min
Preponderance of Transitive Description
Author explains the method of arguing that one's own description is more preponderant than the opponent's counter-description.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of claiming preponderance of a transitive description (wasf muta'addi) over a non-transitive description (wasf qasir) in a usul al-fiqh dispute.

Core Idea
Transitive causes outweigh non-transitive ones because they extend the ruling logically.

When two scholars present competing descriptions (causes) for a ruling, how do you decide which one is stronger? This lesson introduces a key dialectical method from adab al-bahth: showing that your description is more preponderant because it is transitive—it can be extended to other cases—while the opponent's description is confined to its original instance. The author illustrates this with a classic debate between the Maliki/Hanafi and Shafi'i/Hanbali schools on the cause of expiation (kaffarah) for violating the Ramadan fast.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two competing descriptions
In the example, the Maliki/Hanafi propose that the cause of kaffarah is 'violation of the sanctity of Ramadan'. The Shafi'i/Hanbali counter that the cause is 'the specific act of intercourse' because the Prophet explicitly tied kaffarah to it. Write down these two descriptions clearly.
2
Determine transitivity of each description
Check whether each description can logically apply to other acts besides its original instance. 'Violation of sanctity' can apply to eating and drinking as well, so it is transitive (wasf muta'addi). 'Intercourse' is specific to that act, so it is non-transitive (wasf qasir).
3
Apply the preferencing rule
The rule is: a transitive description is more preponderant (arjah) than a non-transitive description, because transitivity (ta'diya) is a recognized preferencing factor in usul al-fiqh. Since the Maliki/Hanafi description is transitive, it outweighs the opponent's non-transitive description.
Consider also that some scholars dispute the validity of a non-transitive cause altogether, further weakening it.
4
Evaluate the conclusion
After applying the rule, the transitive cause prevails. Therefore, expiation (kaffarah) applies not only to intercourse but also to eating and drinking intentionally during Ramadan. This conclusion follows from the stronger description.
The author reminds us that this is just an example, not a full examination of the evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريقة الرابعة : أن يبين رجحان ما ذكره على ما أبداه المعترض ومثاله قول المالكي والحنفي : إن علة كفّارة الجماع في نهار رمضان انتهاك حرمة رمضان، فتجب الكفارة عندهما في الأكل والشرب عمدا كالجماع، فيعارضه الشافعي والحنبلي بخصوص وصف الجماع الذي رتب عليه النبي الله حكم الكفارة، فيجيب المالكي والحنفي بأن الوصف المتعدي إلى غيره أرجح من الوصف القاصر الذي لم يتعد إلى غيره؛ لأن التعدية من المرجحات، وكون العلة هي انتهاك حرمة رمضان يتعدى بها الحكم من الجماع إلى الأكل والشرب بجامع انتهاك حرمة رمضان فتجب الكفارة في الجميع، وكون العلة خصوص الجماع تكون به قاصرة على محلها، فلا يتعدى حكمها إلى شيء، مع أن العلة القاصرة مختلف في التعليل بها أصلاً، كما هو معلوم في محله والقصد مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال .
The fourth method: That he shows the preponderance of what he mentioned over what the objector proposed. And its example: The saying of the Maliki and the Hanafi: That the cause of the expiation for intercourse during the daytime of Ramadan is the violation of the sanctity of Ramadan, so expiation is obligatory according to them for eating and drinking intentionally like intercourse. The Shafi'i and Hanbali oppose him with the specificity of the description of intercourse upon which the Prophet (peace be upon him) attached the ruling of expiation. So the Maliki and Hanafi respond: That the transitive description (al-wasf al-muta'addi) to others is more preponderant than the non-transitive description which does not extend to others; because transitivity is among the preferencing factors. And that the cause is the violation of the sanctity of Ramadan extends the ruling from intercourse to eating and drinking by the common factor of violating the sanctity of Ramadan, so expiation is obligatory in all. And that the cause being the specificity of intercourse makes it non-transitive to its locus, so its ruling does not extend to anything, even though the non-transitive cause is disputed regarding its validity as a cause originally, as is known in its place. And the intent is absolute example, not discussion of the proofs of the schools.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage describes the fourth dialectical method for responding to an objection: demonstrating that one's own description (wasf) carries greater preponderance (rujhan) than the opponent's counter-description. The author provides a concrete example from Islamic law concerning the kaffarah (expiation) for having intercourse during a Ramadan daytime fast. The Maliki and Hanafi schools argue that the cause of the expiation is violating the sanctity of Ramadan. Therefore, they extend the expiation to eating and drinking intentionally in Ramadan, since those also violate the sanctity. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools object, arguing that the cause is specific to the description of intercourse, since the Prophet explicitly tied the expiation to that act. In response, the Maliki and Hanafi use a preferencing principle: a transitive description (wasf muta'addi) that can extend to other cases is stronger than a non-transitive description (wasf qasir) confined to its original case. Transitivity is a recognized preferencing factor (tarjih) in usul al-fiqh. The description 'violation of sanctity' is transitive because it applies to both intercourse and eating/drinking, while the description 'intercourse' is non-transitive and only applies to that act. Moreover, the author adds that the validity of a non-transitive cause is itself contested, making it even weaker. Thus, the transitive cause prevails. The author again reminds us that this is purely an illustration, not a full examination of the schools' evidence.

Key Terms
وصف متعدي (wasf muta'addi): A description (cause) that can be extended to other cases beyond its original instance; transitive cause. [Note: This term is especially important in jadal and usul al-fiqh for analogical extension.]
وصف قاصر (wasf qasir): A description (cause) that is confined to its specific case and does not apply to others; non-transitive cause.
رجحان (rujhan): Preponderance; the quality that makes one description outweigh another in probative value.
تعدية (ta'diya): Transitivity; the extension of a ruling from one case to another based on a common cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Maliki and Hanafi example, why does the description 'violation of the sanctity of Ramadan' outweigh the description 'intercourse' as a cause for kaffarah?
A) Because 'intercourse' is a sin while 'eating' is not.
B) Because 'violation of sanctity' is transitive and can extend to other acts, making it more preponderant.
C) Because the hadith about expiation is weak.
D) Because all scholars agree on the transitive cause.

Key Takeaway
In dialectical argument, a transitive description that extends beyond its original case is more preponderant than a non-transitive description confined to that case.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: Consider the dispute over the cause of the prohibition of riba (usury). The Hanafis argue the 'illah is 'increase in weight or measure in a specific genus', which is transitive to other commodities. The Shafi'is argue the 'illah is 'gold and silver as monetary value', which is non-transitive. Using the method from this lesson, construct a brief argument for which description is more preponderant.
?
Task: In a jadal session, you are defending the position that the 'illah for the obligation of zakat on camels is 'productive wealth' (transitive), while your opponent says it is 'camel ownership' (non-transitive). Write a short response that applies the preferencing rule to show your description outweighs theirs.

The Fourth Method: Showing Preponderance of an Attribute
Author demonstrates the fourth method with a fiqh example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between transitive and limited attributes and apply the preponderance rule to rebut an objection in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
A transitive attribute outweighs a limited one because extension (ta'diya) is a preponderator.

This lesson covers the fourth method of rebuttal in Islamic dialectics: demonstrating that your own attribute is more preponderant than the objector's. The passage uses a classic fiqh example involving the four Sunni schools debating the cause of expiation for intercourse during Ramadan. You will learn how to identify transitive (muta'addī) and limited (qāṣir) attributes and understand why extension gives an attribute greater weight in jadal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conflicting attributes
In the example, the Maliki and Hanafi propose the attribute 'violating the sanctity of Ramadan', while the Shafi'i and Hanbali propose 'the specific attribute of intercourse' as the cause for expiation. Note each side's claimed attribute.
The attributes are the proposed causes ('ilal) for the ruling.
2
Determine which attribute is transitive and which is limited
The attribute 'violating sanctity' can extend to other acts like eating and drinking that also violate the sanctity of Ramadan. The attribute 'specific intercourse' is confined to that act alone and does not apply to other acts.
Transitive means the attribute can be extended to similar cases; limited means it cannot.
3
Apply the preponderance rule
The Maliki and Hanafi argue that the transitive attribute is more preponderant because extension (ta'diya) is a recognized preponderator in usul al-fiqh. This means their cause outweighs the objector's cause.
Preponderance (tarjīḥ) determines which evidence is stronger when conflicting.
4
Recognize the additional weakness of the limited attribute
The author notes that the limited cause is itself controversial; many scholars do not accept a cause that does not extend beyond its original case. This further weakens the objector's position.
This shows that the method not only compares strength but also points out inherent flaws.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريقة الرابعة : أن يبين رجحان ما ذكره على ما أبداه المعترض ومثاله قول المالكي والحنفي : إن علة كفارة الجماع في نهار رمضان انتهاك حرمة رمضان فتجب الكفارة عندهما في الأكل والشرب عمدا كالجماع فيعارضه الشافعي والحنبلي بخصوص وصف الجماع الذي رتب عليه النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم حكم الكفارة فيجيب المالكي والحنفي بأن الوصف المتعدي إلى غيره أرجح من الوصف القاصر الذي لم يتعد إلى غيره لأن التعدية من المرجحات وكون العلة هي انتهاك حرمة رمضان يتعدى بها الحكم من الجماع إلى الأكل والشرب بجامع انتهاك حرمة رمضان فتجب الكفارة في الجميع وكون العلة خصوص الجماع تكون به قاصرة على محلها فلا يتعدى حكمها إلى شيء مع أن العلة القاصرة مختلف في التعليل بها أصلا كما هو معلوم في محله والقصد مطلق المثال لا مناقشة أدلة الأقوال
The fourth method: That he [the arguer] shows the preponderance of what he mentioned over what the objector put forward. Its example is the statement of the Maliki and Hanafi: The cause of the expiation for intercourse during the day of Ramadan is the violation of the sanctity of Ramadan, so expiation is obligatory according to them for eating and drinking deliberately as it is for intercourse. The Shafi'i and Hanbali object by specifying the attribute of intercourse on which the Prophet (peace be upon him) based the ruling of expiation. So the Maliki and Hanafi answer that the attribute that extends to others is more preponderant than the attribute that does not extend to others, because extension (ta'diya) is a preponderator. And that the cause being the violation of the sanctity of Ramadan allows the ruling to be extended from intercourse to eating and drinking due to the shared violation of sanctity, so expiation is obligatory in all. And that the cause being the specific attribute of intercourse makes it limited to its place, so its ruling does not extend to anything, even though the limited cause (al-'illa al-qāsira) is disputed in using it as a cause at all, as is known in its place. And the purpose is simply the example, not a discussion of the evidence of the opinions.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth method of rebuttal in dialectical debate: demonstrating the preponderance (tarjīḥ) of one attribute over another. The author provides a concrete example involving the four Sunni schools of law. The dispute is about the expiation (kaffāra) for intercourse during Ramadan fasting. The Maliki and Hanafi schools argue that the cause is violating the sanctity of Ramadan, so expiation is also required for deliberate eating and drinking. The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools object by limiting the cause to the specific attribute of intercourse as mentioned in the hadith. In response, the Maliki and Hanafi argue that an attribute that is transitive (mut'addī) to other cases is stronger than an attribute that is limited (qāṣir). Extension (ta'diya) is a factor that makes an attribute preponderant. The cause of violating sanctity extends the ruling to eating and drinking, whereas the cause of the specific act of intercourse is confined. The author notes that the limited cause is itself controversial. This example illustrates how one can argue for preponderance in legal reasoning.

Key Terms
الطريقة الرابعة
الرجحان
الوصف المتعدي
الوصف القاصر
التعدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because their cause (violating sanctity) extends to other acts like eating and drinking
C) Because their cause is taken from the Quran
D) Because the Shafi'i and Hanbali have no evidence

Key Takeaway
The fourth method of rebuttal uses tarjīḥ by ta'diya: a transitive attribute outweighs a limited one, providing a systematic way to resolve conflicting 'ilal in jadal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
In a jadal practice session, take the role of the Maliki/Hanafi and script your response to the Shafi'i/Hanbali objection, explicitly invoking extension (ta'diya) as the preponderator.

Denying the Existence of a Claimed Attribute (Man' al-Wujūd)
Author explains how to rebut by denying that the claimed attribute actually exists in the case at hand.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the man' al-wujūd rebuttal in a jadal exchange using a fiqh example.

Core Idea
Man' al-wujūd cuts off an objection by denying the factual existence of the claimed attribute.

In dialectical debates, not every objection is valid on its face. One powerful method is man' al-wujūd: simply denying that the attribute the objector relies on actually exists in the given case. This lesson unpacks how this works using a classic riba-related example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claimed attribute
Read the passage and find what attribute the objector (mu'tarid) is asserting. In the example, the objector claims that 'sustenance and storage' (al-iqtitā' wa al-iddikhār) is measured (makīl).
This attribute is the basis of the objector's analogy or objection.
2
Locate the arguer's denial
The arguer (mudda'i) responds by saying 'We do not concede that it is measured' (la nusallimu annahu makīl). This is the man' al-wujūd: a flat denial that the attribute exists in this case.
The denial is not about the concept, but its instantiation here.
3
Understand the basis for the denial
The arguer provides evidence: the criterion is the custom at the time of the Prophet, and in that era the item was not measured. This grounds the denial in a factual, historical claim.
A man' al-wujūd must be supported; otherwise it is a mere dismissal.
4
Recognise the type of rebuttal
This method differs from conceptual objections (e.g., 'measurement is not the cause'). It attacks the existence of the attribute in reality. It is a factual objection, not a logical one.
In jadal, man' al-wujūd is a valid move when the attribute is not present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريقة الخامسة : منع وجود الوصف الذي عارض به المعترض، مثل أن يعارض الاقتيات والادخار بالكيل، فيقول المستدل : لا نسلم أنه مكيل ؛ لأن العبرة بعادة زمن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم وهو في زمنه غير مكيل
The fifth method: Denying the existence of the attribute with which the objector objected. For example, if he objects [regarding] the principle of food and storage by measurement, the arguer says: 'We do not concede that it is measured; because the consideration is based on the custom of the time of the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him), and in his time it was not measured.'
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth method of rebuttal: denying the existence (man' al-wujūd) of the attribute that the objector claims. The example involves a dispute about whether a certain item is considered measured (makīl) for the purpose of riba. The objector might claim that 'sustenance and storage' (al-iqtitā' wa al-iddikhār) is analogous to measured items. The arguer responds by denying that the item is measured, because the criterion is the custom at the time of the Prophet, and in that era the item was not measured. This method cuts off the objection at its root by negating the factual basis of the attribute.

Key Terms
منع الوجود
المعارضة
الاقتيات والادخار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Because measurement is not a valid cause
D) Because the objector has no evidence

Key Takeaway
Man' al-wujūd is a factual rebuttal that denies the existence of a claimed attribute in a specific case, often grounded in historical or empirical evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Demanding Effectiveness of the Objecting Attribute
Author explains how to demand that the attribute presented by the objector be proven effective in the ruling.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an objector's attribute must be proven effective based on the arguer's method of establishing the cause.

Core Idea
The demand for an attribute's effectiveness depends on the arguer's proof method: appropriateness/similarity requires it; examination/division does not.

In dialectical debates, an arguer can demand that the attribute brought by the objector be effective for the ruling. However, this demand is not always accepted — it depends on how the arguer himself proved his cause. This lesson explains the condition and its rationale.

Guided Path
1
Identify the arguer's method
Determine whether the arguer established his cause using munāsaba (appropriateness), shabah (similarity), sabr wa taqsīm (examination and division), or dawarān (rotation).
2
Apply the condition
If the arguer used munāsaba or shabah, the objector must also show that his attribute is appropriate or similar. If he used sabr wa taqsīm, the objector is not required to prove effectiveness.
Dawarān is treated like sabr wa taqsīm in this regard.
3
Understand the rationale
With munāsaba/shahab, appropriateness is a necessary condition for the cause, so the objector's attribute must also be appropriate. With sabr wa taqsīm, appropriateness is not required for the remaining attribute, so the objection does not stand.
4
Compare similar cases
Note that the rule for dawarān is the same as for sabr wa taqsīm: the objector's attribute does not need to be proven effective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريقة السادسة : مطالبة المعترض بكون الوصف الذي عارض به مؤثرًا في الحكم، وهذا إنما يُسمع من المستدل إذا كان مثبتا للعلة بالمناسبة أو الشبه حتى يحتاج المعارض في معارضته إلى بيان مناسبة أو شبه، بخلاف ما إذا كان مُثبتا للعلة بالسبر والتقسيم، فلا يرد عليه هذا الاعتراض ؛ بناءً على عدم اشتراط المناسبة في الوصف المتبقي في السبر، والظاهر أن الوصف المدار في الدوران كذلك
The sixth method: Demanding from the objector that the attribute with which he objected be effective in the ruling. This is only listened to from the arguer if he established the cause by appropriateness or similarity, so that the objector needs to show appropriateness or similarity in his objection. Unlike if he established the cause by ijtihād/sabar and taqsīm, then this objection does not apply to him, based on the non-requirement of appropriateness in the attribute remaining in the sabar. And it appears that the attribute used in the method of rotation (dawarān) is likewise.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage describes the sixth method of rebuttal: the arguer demands that the attribute brought by the objector be effective (mu'aththir) in the ruling. This demand is accepted only when the arguer himself established the cause using the method of appropriateness (munāsaba) or similarity (shabah), because then the objector's attribute must also be shown to be appropriate or similar. However, if the arguer established the cause using the method of examination and division (sabr wa taqsīm), then this objection is not valid, because in that method appropriateness is not required for the remaining attribute. The same applies to the attribute used in the method of correlation (dawarān). Thus, the preconditions for accepting this objection depend on how the arguer proved his own cause.

Key Terms
المؤثر
المناسبة
الشبه
السبر والتقسيم
الدوران

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) When the arguer established his cause by munāsaba or shabah
C) Always, regardless of how the cause was established
D) Only if the objector uses dawarān

Key Takeaway
The validity of demanding effectiveness from the objector hinges on the arguer's own method of proving the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: Suppose an arguer proves that wine is prohibited due to intoxication using munāsaba. The objector claims it is prohibited because it is a liquid. Should the arguer demand that the objector show the liquid attribute is effective? Explain.
?

Seventh Method: Demonstrating Lack of Precision
Author explains how to rebut by demonstrating that the objector's attribute is imprecise and varies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an opposing attribute in a jadal exchange is imprecise and construct a rebuttal based on its lack of precision.

Core Idea
An imprecise attribute varies with time, person, and situation — it cannot serve as a legal cause.

In dialectical debates over legal causes, an objector may propose an alternative attribute. But if that attribute is not precisely defined (inḍibāṭ), it cannot be a valid cause. This lesson explains the seventh method: showing that the opposing attribute lacks precision, using the example of hardship versus travel for the travel concessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original cause
The arguer claims travel (safar) is the cause for shortening prayer and breaking fast. Note that travel is a precise, well-defined concept in Islamic law.
2
Identify the objector's opposing attribute
The objector objects that the cause is hardship (mashaqqa). Hardship is the proposed alternative attribute.
3
Analyze why the opposing attribute lacks precision
Hardship varies from person to person, from one situation to another, and across different times. Therefore it is not a fixed, measurable attribute and cannot be precisely defined.
4
Construct the rebuttal
The arguer responds that hardship is not precise (lā tanḍabiṭ) and thus cannot serve as a valid legal cause. The original attribute (travel) remains valid because it is precise.
This rebuttal does not prove the original cause is correct, only that the objector's alternative is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريقة السابعة : بيان عدم انضباط الوصف المعارض به، كأن يقول : السفر علة قصر الصلاة وجواز الإفطار في رمضان، فيعارضه المعترض بوصف المشقة، فيجيبُ المستدل بأن المشقة لا تنضبط ؛ لاختلافها باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال والأزمنة. ومنها غير ذلك تركناه اختصارًا لأن فيما ذكرنا كفاية
The seventh method: Showing that the attribute used by the objector is not precise (unḍabiṭ). For example, if he says: 'Travel is the cause for shortening prayer and allowing breaking the fast in Ramadan,' and the objector objects with the attribute of hardship (mashaqqa), the arguer responds that hardship is not precise, because it varies with different persons, conditions, and times. And there are other [methods] besides these, which we have omitted for brevity, because what we have mentioned is sufficient.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage presents the seventh method of rebuttal: demonstrating that the objector's attribute lacks precision (inḍibāṭ). The example is about the cause for the concessions of shortening prayer and breaking fast during travel. If someone claims the cause is travel itself, and the objector objects by saying the cause is hardship (mashaqqa), the arguer can respond that hardship is not a well-defined attribute because it varies from person to person, situation to situation, and time to time. Therefore, it cannot be a valid legal cause. The author then concludes by noting that there are additional methods not covered, but the ones presented are sufficient. This method guards against using vague and subjective attributes as legal causes.

Key Terms
الانضباط
المشقة
السفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The seventh method rebuts an opposing attribute by showing it lacks precision (inḍibāṭ) because it varies across different contexts.

Classification of Destructive Criticisms in Dialectic
Author categorizes six questions posed to the arguer in usul al-fiqh, five of which are destructive criticisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish istifsār from the five qawādiḥ and identify each destructive criticism by name in a dialectical exchange.

Core Idea
Six questions, five destroy arguments.

In usul al-fiqh, when a jurist presents an argument, the opponent may respond with one of six standard questions. One of them merely seeks clarification; the other five are designed to expose flaws and break the argument. This lesson lays out that classification, giving you the basic toolkit for analyzing dialectical exchanges.

Guided Path
1
Identify the six questions
The author lists six questions: istifsār, naqḍ, kasr, man', qalb, and mu'āraḍa. Note that one is not destructive.
2
Separate the non-destructive one
Istifsār is a request for explanation—it does not damage the argument. The other five are called qawādiḥ (destructive criticisms).
3
Memorize the five qawādiḥ
The five are: naqḍ (refutation by counterexample), kasr (breaking by inconsistency), man' (denial of a premise), qalb (reversal), and mu'āraḍa (counterargument).
4
Note the subclass relationship
The author points out that qalb is actually a type of mu'āraḍa, but usul scholars treat them as separate destructive criticisms for precision.
This shows the terminological nuance in the discipline.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا ذكرنا فيما مضى تطبيق ستة أسئلة الأسئلة من التي ترد على المستدل في الأصول واحد منها ليس بقادح في نفس الأمر وهو الاستفسار وخمسة من القوادح وهي : النقض والكسر والمنع والقلب والمعارضة وإن كان القلب نوعا من المعارضة لأن الأصوليين يعدونه قادحا مستقلا ويعدون المعارضة قادحا آخر مستقلا
Know that we have previously mentioned the application of six questions that are directed at the arguer in usul [al-fiqh]. One of them is not a destructive criticism in itself, and that is istifsār (request for explanation). And five are destructive criticisms: al-naqḍ (refutation), al-kasr (breaking), al-man' (denial), al-qalb (reversal), and al-mu'āraḍa (counterargument), even though reversal is a type of counterargument; because the uṣūliyyūn consider it an independent destructive criticism, and they consider counterargument another independent one.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of six questions that are raised against an arguer (mustadill) in the discipline of uṣūl al-fiqh. The author states that one of them, istifsār (seeking clarification), is not a destructive criticism (qādiḥ) in itself; the other five are destructive. The five are: naqḍ (refutation by citing a contradictory case), kasr (breaking the argument by showing its inconsistency), man' (denial of a premise), qalb (reversing the argument), and mu'āraḍa (presenting a counterargument). The author notes that although qalb is essentially a type of mu'āraḍa, the uṣūli scholars treat it as a separate destructive criticism. This classification is important for understanding the tools of objection in dialectical debate.

Key Terms
القادح
الاستفسار
النقض
الكسر
المنع
القلب
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, which of the six questions is not a destructive criticism (qādiḥ)?
A) al-naqḍ
B) al-kasr
C) al-istifsār
D) al-qalb

Key Takeaway
The six questions to an arguer include one non-destructive (istifsār) and five destructive criticisms (naqḍ, kasr, man', qalb, mu'āraḍa), with qalb being a subtype of mu'āraḍa.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider this exchange: 'Why do you claim that ribā is prohibited by analogy to gold? Because both are measures of weight.' Opponent replies: 'But barley is also a measure of weight, yet it is permitted to trade it with excess.' Which destructive criticism (qādiḥ) is the opponent using? Explain.
?
In a debate, an opponent says: 'I deny that your premise that every intoxicant is forbidden is correct.' Which of the six questions is this? Is it destructive?

The Sixth Method of Objection: Demanding Effectiveness of the Objecting Attribute
Author describes the sixth method of objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when an objector's demand for effectiveness of his conflicting attribute is valid, based on how the proponent established the cause.

Core Idea
The objector's demand for effectiveness only applies when the proponent used suitability or similarity, not sabr wa taqsim.

In dialectical debate, objectors often challenge the proponent's cause by presenting a conflicting attribute. The sixth method specifies that the objector must demand that his attribute be effective in the ruling, but this demand is only heard if the proponent established his cause through suitability (munasaba) or similarity (shabh). If the proponent used examination and division (sabr wa taqsim), the objection is not applicable. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correct argumentation in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's method of establishing the cause
Examine how the proponent (mustadil) justified the link between the attribute and the ruling. Is it by suitability (munasaba), similarity (shabh), or examination and division (sabr wa taqsim)? This determines the objector's next move.
2
Determine the objector's demand
The objector (mu'tarid) demands that his own conflicting attribute be effective (mu'aththir) in the ruling. This demand is formalized as: 'Prove that your attribute (the one you object with) actually influences the ruling.'
3
Assess the validity of the demand based on the proponent's method
If the proponent used munasaba or shabh, the demand is valid – the objector must then provide evidence for suitability or similarity. If the proponent used sabr wa taqsim, the demand is invalid because suitability is not required in that method.
The text explicitly states: 'بخلاف ما إذا كان مُثبتا للعلة بالسبر والتقسيم، فلا يرد عليه هذا الاعتراض'
4
Apply the rule to a hypothetical exchange
Imagine a debate where the proponent claims that a specific attribute (e.g., 'nourishing and storing') is the cause for a ruling (e.g., prohibition of riba). The objector objects with a different attribute (e.g., 'being measured by volume'). The proponent must check his own method: if he used munasaba, the objector's demand is valid; if he used sabr, it is not.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثل أن يعارض الاقتيات والادخار بالكيل، فيقول المستدل : لا نسلم أنه مكيل ؛ لأن العبرة بعادة زمن رسول الله له ، وهو في زمنه غير مكيل . الطريقة السادسة : مطالبة المعترض بكون الوصف الذي عارض به مؤثرًا في الحكم، وهذا إنما يُسمع من المستدل إذا كان مثبتا للعلة بالمناسبة أو الشبه حتى يحتاج المعارض في معارضته إلى بيان مناسبة أو شبه، بخلاف ما إذا كان مُثبتا للعلة بالسبر والتقسيم، فلا يرد عليه هذا الاعتراض ؛ بناءً على عدم اشتراط المناسبة في الوصف المتبقي في السبر، والظاهر أن الوصف المدار في الدوران كذلك . الطريقة السابعة : بيان عدم انضباط الوصف المعارض به، كأن يقول : السفر علة قصر الصلاة وجواز الإفطار في رمضان، فيعارضه المعترض بوصف المشقة، فيجيبُ المستدل بأن المشقة لا تنضبط ؛ لاختلافها باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال والأزمنة. ومنها غير ذلك تركناه اختصارًا لأن فيما ذكرنا كفاية .
For example, one might object to [the use of] 'nourishing and storing' with [the criterion of] being measured by volume, and the proponent says: 'We do not accept that it is measured by volume; because the consideration is the custom at the time of the Messenger of God ﷺ, and it was not measured by volume in his time.' The sixth method: The objector's demand that the attribute with which he objects be effective in the ruling. This is only heard from the proponent if he had established the cause by suitability (munasaba) or similarity (shabh), so that the objector in his objection needs to explain suitability or similarity. Contrary to if he had established the cause by examination and division (sabr wa taqsim), then this objection does not apply to him, based on the non-requirement of suitability in the remaining attribute in sabr, and apparently the attribute that operates in rotation is likewise. The seventh method: Showing that the objecting attribute is not well-defined. For example, [the proponent] says: Travel is the cause for shortening prayer and the permissibility of breaking fast in Ramadan. The objector objects with the attribute of hardship. The proponent responds that hardship is not well-defined because it varies with different persons, conditions, and times. And there are other methods which we have left out for brevity, for what we have mentioned is sufficient.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage explains two specific methods of objection in dialectical debate. The sixth method is the objector's demand that the attribute he uses to object must be effective in the ruling. The author clarifies that this demand is only heard if the proponent established the cause via suitability or similarity. If the cause was established by sabr wa taqsim, the objection does not apply because suitability is not required. The seventh method involves showing that the objecting attribute is not well-defined, using the example of travel as a cause for shortening prayer. The objector objects with 'hardship,' but the proponent responds that hardship varies and is not a fixed attribute. The author concludes by stating that these methods suffice and others are omitted.

Key Terms
المناسبة
الشبه
السبر والتقسيم
الوصف المعارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth method, when is the objector's demand for effectiveness of his conflicting attribute heard?
A) When the proponent established the cause by sabr wa taqsim.
B) When the proponent established the cause by munasaba or shabh.
C) Always, regardless of how the cause was established.
D) Never, it is not a valid objection.

Key Takeaway
The validity of an objector's demand for effectiveness depends on the proponent's method of establishing the cause: it is valid only with suitability or similarity, not with sabr wa taqsim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Review a juristic debate where the proponent claims 'travel is the cause for the permission to shorten prayer' using the method of sabr wa taqsim. The objector counters with 'hardship is the cause.' Apply the sixth method to determine if the objector's demand for effectiveness is valid. Justify your answer.
?
Construct a brief munazara exchange where the proponent uses munasaba to establish a cause. Then have the objector present a conflicting attribute and demand effectiveness. Show how the proponent should respond according to the sixth method.

The Seventh Method of Objection: Showing the Objecting Attribute is Not Well-Defined
Author describes the seventh method of objection: showing the objecting attribute is not well-defined.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when an objecting attribute lacks well-definedness (indibat) and use this as a valid objection in munazara.

Core Idea
An objection based on an attribute that varies and is not well-defined can be refuted by showing its lack of indibat.

The seventh method of objection involves demonstrating that the attribute proposed by the objector is not well-defined (ghayr mundabit). A well-defined attribute is one that is fixed and does not vary across persons, conditions, or times. If the attribute is not well-defined, it cannot serve as a reliable cause. The author illustrates this with the classic example of travel as a cause for shortening prayer, where the objector suggests hardship as an alternative cause, but hardship is rejected because it varies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objecting attribute
Locate the attribute (wasf) that the objector presents as an alternative cause. In the example, it is 'hardship' (mashaqqa).
2
Check the attribute for well-definedness (indibat)
A well-defined attribute must be fixed, not varying. Ask: Does this attribute change depending on persons, conditions, or times? If yes, it lacks indibat.
The text states: 'المشقة لا تنضبط لاختلافها باختلاف الأشخاص والأحوال والأزمنة'
3
Formulate the objection based on lack of indibat
The proponent argues that the objecting attribute is not well-defined, so it cannot be a valid cause. The objection is not against the attribute's effectiveness but against its instability.
4
Apply to the travel example
The proponent claims travel is the cause for shortening prayer. The objector says hardship is the real cause. The proponent counters: 'Hardship is not well-defined because it varies; travel, however, is well-defined.' This demonstrates the seventh method.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Contrary to if he had established the cause by examination and division (sabr wa taqsim), then this objection does not apply to him, based on the non-requirement of suitability in the remaining attribute in sabr, and apparently the attribute that operates in rotation is likewise. The seventh method: Showing that the objecting attribute is not well-defined. Classification of Six Questions in Usul al-Fiqh
Author classifies the six types of questions that can be put to the proponent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the six types of questions in usul al-fiqh and distinguish which are defects.

Core Idea
Not every question is an attack; clarification is neutral.

In usul al-fiqh debates, the opponent can raise questions against the proponent's argument. This passage classifies six such questions, one of which is not a defect. Understanding this classification is essential for effective dialectical training.

Guided Path
1
Identify the non-defect
Locate the question that is not a defect: istifsar (inquiry for clarification). The author explicitly says it is not a defect in itself.
Istifsar seeks explanation without challenging the argument.
2
List the five defects
Find the five questions that are defects: naqd (inconsistency), kasr (break), man' (prevention), qalb (reversal), and mu'arada (counter-objection).
These are the 'qawadih' (defects) that attack the argument.
3
Understand the relationship between qalb and mu'arada
Note the author's remark that qalb is a type of mu'arada, but usul scholars treat them as independent defects.
Qalb uses the proponent's own reasoning to derive the opposite ruling.
4
Recognize the context
These questions are used in dialectical exchanges in usul al-fiqh. The proponent must respond appropriately to each type.
Knowing the type helps formulate the correct response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أنا ذكرنا فيما مضى تطبيق ستة أسئلة الأسئلة من التي ترد على المستدل في الأصول واحد منها ليس بقادح في نفس الأمر وهو الاستفسار وخمسة من القوادح وهي : النقض، والكسر، والمنع، والقلب ،والمعارضة وإن كان القلب نوعًا من المعارضة؛ لأن الأصوليين يعدونه قادحًا مستقلاً، ويعدون المعارضة قادحًا آخر مستقلاً .
Know that we previously mentioned the application of six questions that are presented to the proponent in [the science of] principles. One of them is not a defect in itself, namely istifsar (inquiry for clarification), and five are defects: naqd (inconsistency), kasr (break), man' (prevention), qalb (reversal), and mu'arada (counter-objection). Even though qalb is a type of mu'arada, the usul scholars count it as an independent defect and count mu'arada as another independent defect.
Source pages p. 328

Commentary
This passage is a classification. The author reminds the reader that six types of questions can be directed at the proponent in usul al-fiqh. One of these, istifsar, is not a genuine defect because it merely seeks clarification. The other five are actual defects: naqd (showing inconsistency in the proponent's argument), kasr (breaking the causal link), man' (denying a premise), qalb (reversing the argument, i.e., using the same reasoning to derive a contradictory ruling), and mu'arada (presenting a counter-argument based on another attribute). The author notes that qalb is a form of mu'arada but is treated separately by usul scholars.

Key Terms
الاستفسار
النقض
الكسر
المنع
القلب
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) al-man'
B) al-istifsar
C) al-naqd
D) al-mu'arada

Key Takeaway
In dialectic, the six questions include one non-defective clarification and five defects that attack the argument; knowing each is crucial for proper debate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Mu'arada Bil-Mithl: Using Parallel Categorical Syllogisms to Oppose an Argument
Author explains mu'arada bil-mithl with parallel categorical syllogisms on riba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a mu'arada bil-mithl by forming a parallel categorical syllogism with the same structure but different terms to yield a contradictory conclusion.

Core Idea
Mu'arada bil-mithl mirrors the original syllogism to force a contradiction, assuming a single valid cause.

In dialectical debate (jadal), opponents often respond by constructing a parallel argument that mirrors the form of the original but leads to an opposite ruling. The author illustrates this with a classic usul al-fiqh example on riba. In this lesson, you will learn how mu'arada bil-mithl works and how to recognize it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original argument (mudda'i)
Locate the Hanafi proponent's claim: 'This is measured by volume, and every measured-by-volume item involves riba; therefore this involves riba.' Note that the cause ('illa) is volume, and the principle of man' ta'addud al-'ilal prevents alternative causes.
2
Extract the categorical syllogism structure
Write the two premises and conclusion: Major premise: 'Every measured-by-volume item is ribawi.' Minor premise: 'This is measured by volume.' Conclusion: 'This is ribawi.' This is a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani).
3
Analyze the opponent's counter-argument (mu'arada bil-mithl)
The Shafi'i opponent constructs a parallel syllogism with different terms: 'Apples are food items (mat'um), and every food item involves riba, even if not measured; therefore apples involve riba.' This yields a conclusion that contradicts the implication of the original (that only measured items are ribawi).
Both syllogisms are categorical (first figure). They differ in the subject matter (makil vs mat'um) but share the same logical form.
4
Understand why the contradiction arises
Due to the principle of man' ta'addud al-'ilal, if the cause is volume, then food items not measured cannot be ribawi. The opponent's syllogism implies that the cause is edibility (mat'um), not volume. Since both cannot be true, the arguments stand in opposition.
5
Apply the concept to a new example
Practice constructing a mu'arada bil-mithl for a different fiqh issue, such as zakat on livestock, using the same method of mirroring the syllogistic form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما النوعان الآخران من المعارضة، اللذان هما المعارضة في الأصل، والمعارضة في الفرع فيصح تطبيقهما على المعارضة بالمثل والمعارضة بالغير في البحث والمناظرة.
وإيضاح ذلك أن يقول المستدل : هذا مكيل، وكل مكيل يحرم فيه الربا؛ لأن علة تحريم الربا الكيل [والفرض] منع تعدد العلل المستنبطة كما تقدم، ينتج : هذا يحرم فيه الربا، ولازم هذا التعليل أن المطعوم إن كان غير مكيل لا يحرم فيه الربا؛ لمنع تعدد العلل المستنبطة.
فيعارضه الشافعي مثلاً معارضة بالمثل فيقول : التفاح مطعوم، وكل مطعوم يحرم فيه الربا وإن كان غير مكيل، ينتج : التفاح يحرم فيه الربا، ولازم هذا التعليل أنه لا يحرم في غير المطعوم، لمنع تعدد العلل المستنبطة، وهو مناقض لما دل عليه دليل الأول، فمقتضى دليل الأول أنه يحرم في المكيل ولا يحرم في مطعوم غير مكيل، ومقتضى دليل الثاني أنه يحرم في كل مطعوم وإن كان غير مكيل، ولا يحرم في مكيل غير مطعوم، فكل من الدليلين قياس اقتراني، وكلاهما يستلزم نقيض الآخر، لما ذكرنا أن هذا النوع من المعارضة مبني على منع تعدد العلل المستنبطة، فوجود علة يلزمه نفي غيرها، كما أوضحنا بالأمثلة.
ويصح أن يكون من المعارضة بالغير، كأن يقول الحنفي: الذرة مكيلة، وكل مكيل ربوي ينتج الذرة ربوية . وهذا الدليل اقتراني . فيعارضه الشافعي معارضة بالغير بدليل استثنائي فيقول : لو كان كل مكيل ربويا - أي [والفرض] أنه لا ربوي غير المكيل ؛ لمنع تعدد العلل المستنبطة - لكان ملء الكف من البر غير ربوي؛ لأنه غير مكيل لقلته لكنه ربوي ينتج ما كل مكيل ربويا وهو إبطال بالمعارضة بالغير.
As for the other two types of opposition, which are opposition in the root (al-asl) and opposition in the branch (al-far'), it is valid to apply them to opposition by the like (bil-mithl) and opposition by the other (bil-ghayr) in inquiry and debate. Clarification of that: the proponent says: 'This is measured by volume (makil), and every measured-by-volume item involves usury (riba) because the cause ('illa) of prohibition of usury is volume, and [the assumption] is the prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes (man' ta'addud al-'ilal al-mustanbata) as previously stated.' This yields: 'This involves usury.' And the necessary consequence of this reasoning is that food items that are not measured by volume do not involve usury, due to the prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes. Then the opponent (e.g., al-Shafi'i) opposes him with opposition by the like, saying: 'Apples are food items (mat'um), and every food item involves usury even if not measured by volume.' This yields: 'Apples involve usury.' And the necessary consequence of this reasoning is that it does not involve usury in what is not a food item, due to the prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes. This contradicts what the first proof indicated. So the implication of the first proof is that it involves usury in what is measured by volume and does not involve usury in a food item not measured by volume; the implication of the second proof is that it involves usury in every food item even if not measured by volume, and does not involve usury in what is measured by volume but is not a food item. Thus each of the two proofs is a categorical syllogism (qiyas iqtirani), and each entails the negation of the other, because as we mentioned this type of opposition is built on the prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes. The existence of a cause necessitates the negation of others, as we clarified with examples. And it may be opposition by the other, such as when the Hanafi says: 'Corn is measured by volume, and every measured-by-volume item is usurious (ribawi),' yielding: 'Corn is usurious.' This proof is categorical. Then the opponent opposes him by the other with a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i), saying: 'If every measured-by-volume item were usurious — i.e., [and the assumption] is that there is no usurious item other than the measured-by-volume, due to the prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes — then a handful of wheat would not be usurious because it is not measured by volume due to its small quantity; but it is usurious.' This yields: 'Not every measured-by-volume item is usurious.' And this is an invalidation by opposition by the other.
Source pages p. 329

Commentary
This passage explains how two types of mu'arada (opposition/counter-argument) in dialectic—mu'arada bil-mithl and mu'arada bil-ghayr—can be applied to the concepts of opposition in the root (al-asl) and opposition in the branch (al-far'). The author illustrates with examples from the topic of riba (usury) in Islamic jurisprudence. First, the Hanafi proponent argues that usury is prohibited in items measured by volume (makil) because the 'illa (cause) is volume itself, assuming prevention of multiplicity of deduced causes (man' ta'addud al-'ilal). This yields that only measured items are usurious. The Shafi'i opponent then uses mu'arada bil-mithl by proposing a parallel argument: everything edible (mat'um) involves usury, regardless of being measured. This leads to a contradictory conclusion—that some measured items (e.g., minerals) might not be usurious, and some non-measured edibles (e.g., apples) are usurious. Both arguments are categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani), and they negate each other because they are built on the principle that a single cause excludes others. Then the author presents mu'arada bil-ghayr: the Hanafi uses a categorical syllogism claiming corn is usurious because it is measured. The Shafi'i counters with a conditional syllogism (qiyas istithna'i): if every measured item were usurious (and only measured items are usurious due to prevention of multiplicity), then a small handful of wheat (not measured by volume due to its small quantity) would not be usurious—but it is usurious in Islamic law. This refutes the original claim. The key point is that mu'arada bil-mithl uses a counterpart argument with the same structure but different terms, while mu'arada bil-ghayr uses a different type of argument (conditional) to show a counterexample.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المعارضة بالمثل
منع تعدد العلل المستنبطة
قياس اقتراني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the bil-mithl example, what is the structural relationship between the Hanafi and Shafi'i arguments?
A) The Shafi'i argument uses a conditional syllogism while the Hanafi uses categorical.
B) Both are categorical syllogisms with the same form but different terms.
C) The Shafi'i argument affirms the consequent of the Hanafi's conditional.
D) They are identical in both form and content.

Key Takeaway
Mu'arada bil-mithl is a dialectical tool where a parallel categorical syllogism is used to generate a contradictory conclusion, relying on the premise that only one cause is valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a mu'arada bil-mithl on a different fiqh issue: e.g., the Hanbali argument that every intoxicant is haram because intoxication is the cause; use mu'arada bil-mithl to argue that every substance causing drowsiness is haram, even if not intoxicating, to show a contradiction.
