The Four Types of Dialectical Objections
Author defines four types of objections and discusses their interrelations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of objections (naqd, fasad al-wad', qalb, munasaba) based on the presence of a contrary and the means by which it is established.

Core Idea
Four objections are distinguished by how the contrary is established: with the description, by the description, by both premises, or without a contrary.

In dialectical disputations, objections (qadih) are carefully classified. This passage from Adab al-Bahth defines four types based on how a contrary (manafi) is related to the opponent's evidence. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for both mounting and countering attacks in munazara.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four objection types
Find the names naqd, fasad al-wad', qalb, and munasaba in the Arabic passage. Note that each is defined in relation to whether a contrary (manafi) exists and how it is established.
2
Analyze the defining features
For each type, determine how the contrary is established: (1) naqd: the contrary exists with the description (wasf); (2) fasad al-wad': the contrary is proven by the description itself; (3) qalb: the contrary is proven by both the description and the opponent's premise (asl); (4) munasaba: there is no contrary, but the suitability of the description for the ruling is denied.
3
Apply the summary formula
Read the Arabic summary: 'ثبوت المنافي مع الوصف نقض، فإن زِيدَ ثبوته بالوصف فهو فساد الوضع، وإن زيد كونه به وبأصل المستدل فقلب، وبدون ثبوته معه فالمناسبة.' This concisely captures the distinctions.
4
Examine the munasaba condition
Note that munasaba only applies when both opponents share the same perspective (وجهة نظر). The example of Hanafi vs. others on zakat timing illustrates this: the Hanafi looks at leniency for the owner, while the other looks at the poor's need. Since perspectives differ, a munasaba objection fails.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما ينافي الحكم بأصل المستدل وهذا يثبته بأصل آخر، فلو ذكره بأصله لكان هو القلب .
وفساد الوضع أيضًا يشبه القدح في المناسبة من حيث إن موجه القدح به نافٍ لمناسبة الوصف للحكم بمناسبة الوصف لنقيض الحكم، إلا أنه لا يقصد هنا بيان عدم مناسبة الوصف للحكم، بل بناء نقيض الحكم عليه في أصل آخر، فلو بين مناسبته لنقيض الحكم بلا أصل لكان قدحًا في المناسبة .
فتحصل مما ذكرنا أن ثبوت المنافي مع الوصف نقض، فإن زِيدَ ثبوته بالوصف فهو فساد الوضع، وإن زيد كونه به وبأصل المستدل فقلب وبدون ثبوته معه فالمناسبة .
ومعلوم أن القدح بالمناسبة لا يكون إلا إذا كانت وجهة نظر كل من الخصمين متحدة، فإن اختلفت فلا قدح بعدم المناسبة، كنظر الحنفي إلى الرفق بالمالك في قوله بالتراخي في الزكاة، ونظر غيره إلى حاجة المسكين في قوله بوجوب الزكاة على الفور، ونحو ذلك .
والظاهر أن فساد الوضع بالنسبة إلى البحث والمناظرة يمكن رجوعه إلى النقض، لأن النقض في البحث والمناظرة شامل لكل تخلف للمدلول عن دليله وما يدل على التغليظ يتخلف عنه الحكم بالتخفيف، إلى آخر الأقسام.
ويمكن رجوعه إلى المعارضة؛ لأن المقتضي للتغليظ مثلاً المستدل به على التخفيف معارض بأن التخفيف يستلزم عدم مقتضي التغليظ ، فكأن المعارض يقول : دليلك هذا مقتض للتغليظ ، ولا شيء من مقتضي التغليظ يقتضي التخفيف، ينتج : دليلك هذا لا يقتضي التخفيف، وهذه النتيجة نقيض الدعوى المستدل عليها .
What contradicts the ruling on the basis of the opponent's premise — and this (the opponent) establishes it by another premise — so if he had mentioned it on his own premise, it would be qalb (inversion). And fasad al-wad' (corruption of placement) also resembles qadh (objection) in munasaba (suitability) in that the one directing the objection by it negates the suitability of the description for the ruling by the suitability of the description for the opposite of the ruling, except that here it is not intended to show the lack of suitability of the description for the ruling, but rather to build the opposite of the ruling upon it in another premise. So if he showed its suitability for the opposite of the ruling without a premise, it would be qadh in munasaba. So it becomes clear from what we have mentioned that the existence of a contrary (manafi) with the description is naqd (contradiction). Then if its existence with the description is added, it is fasad al-wad'. If its existence with it and with the opponent's premise is added, it is qalb. And without its existence with it, it is munasaba. And it is known that objection by munasaba only occurs when the viewpoints of both opponents are unified. If they differ, then there is no objection due to lack of suitability, like the view of the Hanafi on leniency towards the owner in his saying of postponement (tarakhi) in zakat, and the view of another on the need of the poor in his saying of the obligation of zakat immediately, and the like. And it appears that fasad al-wad' with respect to research and disputation can be reduced to naqd, because naqd in research and disputation includes every case where the implication falls short of its evidence, and what indicates severity is followed by the ruling of leniency, and so on for the other divisions. And it can be reduced to mu'arada (opposition), because what necessitates severity, for example, used as evidence for leniency is opposed by the fact that leniency entails the absence of what necessitates severity, so it is as if the opponent says: this evidence of yours necessitates severity, and nothing that necessitates severity necessitates leniency, resulting in: this evidence does not necessitate leniency, and this result is the opposite of the claim being argued for.
Source pages p. 337

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical analysis classifying types of objections (qadih) used in disputation. The author begins by defining qalb (inversion): when the opponent establishes a contrary (manafi) to the ruling using another premise, but if he had used his own original premise, it would be qalb. Then fasad al-wad' is compared to munasaba: the objector negates the suitability of the description for the ruling by showing its suitability for the opposite, but here the aim is not to show lack of suitability but to build the opposite on another premise. If he did so without a premise, it would be a mere objection in munasaba. The author then summarizes: existence of a contrary with the description is naqd; adding that the contrary is established by the description itself makes it fasad al-wad'; adding that it is by the description and the opponent's premise makes it qalb; without such existence, it is munasaba. Importantly, munasaba is valid only when both parties share the same perspective (viewpoint); if they differ, no objection from lack of suitability arises. An example is given: the Hanafi looks at leniency for the owner in postponing zakat, while another looks at the poor's need for immediate payment—these do not conflict because perspectives differ. Finally, the author suggests that fasad al-wad' can be reduced either to naqd (since naqd covers any failure of the implication to match its evidence) or to mu'arada (opposition), by constructing a syllogism showing that the evidence necessarily leads to the opposite of the claim. This illustrates the nuanced classification of argumentative flaws in classical Islamic dialectic.

Key Terms
النقض
فساد الوضع
القلب
المناسبة
المنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes fasad al-wad' from naqd?
A) Fasad al-wad' uses a different premise from the opponent's original.
B) In fasad al-wad', the contrary is established by the description itself, not just coexisting with it.
C) Fasad al-wad' is only applicable in munasaba objections.
D) Fasad al-wad' requires a unified viewpoint between opponents.

Key Takeaway
The four objections are distinguished by how the contrary relates to the description and the opponent's premise: coexisting, proven by it, proven by both, or absent (with perspective condition).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For a hypothetical debate: The opponent argues that 'wine is prohibited because it intoxicates.' The claimant objects by pointing out that 'nabidh intoxicates but is not considered prohibited by some.' Which type of objection is this (naqd, fasad al-wad', qalb, or munasaba)? Explain using the definitions from the passage.
?
Consider a debate about the ruling on eating pork. The proposer says: 'Anything harmful is prohibited; pork is harmful, so it is prohibited.' The objector says: 'But some harmful things are not prohibited, like medicine.' What type of objection? Is it a reduction of the description? (Answer: naqd, because a contrary exists with the description 'harmful' without being proven by the description itself).

Reducing Fasad al-Wad': Can It Be Subsumed Under Naqd or Mu'arada?
Author discusses possible reductions of fasad al-wad'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a fasad al-wad' objection and determine whether it can be reduced to naqd or mu'arada based on the context of research and disputation.

Core Idea
Fasad al-wad' can be reduced to either naqd (by showing the evidence fails) or mu'arada (by constructing a syllogism against it).

The author notes that fasad al-wad' is not always a distinct type; in the context of formal debate (bahth wa munazara), it can be seen as a form of naqd or mu'arada. This flexibility shows the practical integration of classification and deepens our understanding of dialectical strategy.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of fasad al-wad'
Fasad al-wad' is when a contrary (manafi) to the opponent's ruling is established by the description itself (wasf). This means the description, which the opponent uses to support the ruling, actually supports the opposite ruling.
2
Read the passage on reduction
Focus on: 'والظاهر أن فساد الوضع بالنسبة إلى البحث والمناظرة يمكن رجوعه إلى النقض... ويمكن رجوعه إلى المعارضة'. This states that fasad al-wad' can be reduced to naqd or mu'arada.
3
Understand the reduction to naqd
The author explains that naqd in research and disputation includes every case where the implication (madlul) falls short of its evidence. Fasad al-wad' similarly shows that the evidence fails to support the claim, so it reduces to naqd.
4
Understand the reduction to mu'arada
The author provides an example: if the opponent uses evidence that necessitates severity (taghliz) to argue for leniency (takhfif), the objector can construct a syllogism: 'This evidence necessitates severity; nothing that necessitates severity necessitates leniency; therefore this evidence does not necessitate leniency.' This is mu'arada (opposition) because the objector opposes the evidence with a counter-argument.
5
Evaluate the implications
Consider whether these reductions make fasad al-wad' redundant. The author does not argue it is useless, but shows that the same logical flaw can be described in multiple ways, providing flexibility in debate.

Source Text

Reducing an Objection to Opposition (Mu'āraḍa)
Author illustrates how an objection implying strictness is countered by showing it reduces to opposition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and reconstruct the method of mu'āraḍa (opposition) in a dialectical exchange, showing how an opponent counters a claim by deriving its contradictory from the proponent's own evidence.

Core Idea
Opposition turns the opponent's evidence against him to derive a contradiction.

In dialectical argumentation, it is often not enough to simply reject an opponent's claim; one must show it leads to a contradiction. This passage introduces a powerful technique—mu'āraḍa—where the opponent takes the proponent's own evidence and demonstrates that it implies the opposite of what the proponent claims. Let's see how this works step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and evidence
Find the claim (mudda'a) the proponent is trying to prove and the evidence (muqtaḍī) he adduces. In the example, the proponent argues that a certain factor implies leniency (takhfīf).
The evidence is something that supposedly requires or implies the claim.
2
Determine what the evidence actually implies
The opponent reinterprets the evidence to show it implies something else—here, strictness (taghlīẓ). This is the core of mu'āraḍa: finding an alternative implication.
The alternative implication must be something that contradicts the original claim.
3
Construct the opponent's syllogism
Formally: 'This evidence requires strictness. Nothing that requires strictness implies leniency. Therefore, this evidence does not imply leniency.' This is a categorical syllogism in the first figure (Barbara).
The conclusion is the negation of what the proponent wanted to prove.
4
Recognize the contradiction
The conclusion 'the evidence does not imply leniency' directly contradicts the proponent's original claim. This refutes the proponent's argument by showing its own evidence leads to the opposite.
This is a form of argument by contradiction, an immediate inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالتخفيف، إلى آخر الأقسام. ويمكن رجوعه إلى المعارضة؛ لأن المقتضي للتغليظ مثلاً المستدل به على التخفيف معارض بأن التخفيف يستلزم عدم مقتضي التغليظ ، فكأن المعارض يقول : دليلك هذا مقتض للتغليظ ، ولا شيء من مقتضي التغليظ يقتضي التخفيف، ينتج : دليلك هذا لا يقتضي التخفيف، وهذه النتيجة نقيض الدعوى المستدل عليها .
...with leniency, up to the rest of the divisions. And it is possible to refer it back to opposition; because the factor requiring strictness, for example, which is adduced by the proponent for leniency, is opposed by [the fact] that leniency entails the absence of the factor requiring strictness. So it is as if the opponent says: 'This evidence of yours requires strictness, and nothing that requires strictness implies leniency.' Therefore it follows: 'This evidence of yours does not imply leniency.' And this conclusion is the contradictory of the claim for which the evidence was adduced.
Source pages p. 338

Commentary
This passage explains a dialectical technique known as reducing an objection to opposition (mu'arada). The author presents an example: if the proponent argues that a certain factor implies leniency, the opponent can counter by showing that the same factor actually implies strictness. The opponent then constructs a syllogism: the evidence requires strictness; nothing that requires strictness implies leniency; therefore, the evidence does not imply leniency. This conclusion contradicts the proponent's original claim, effectively refuting it. The method is a form of argument by contradiction common in Islamic jurisprudence and logic.

Key Terms
المعارضة
المقتضي
نقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example from the passage, what is the conclusion of the opponent's syllogism?
A) The evidence requires strictness.
B) The evidence implies leniency.
C) The evidence does not imply leniency.
D) The evidence is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
Mu'āraḍa is a dialectical technique where the opponent uses the proponent's own evidence to derive a conclusion that contradicts the original claim, effectively refuting it by immediate inference.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task: In a debate about whether a particular act is wajib or mandub, the proponent argues that because the act involves risk of harm, it must be wajib (to avoid harm). The opponent wants to use mu'āraḍa. Construct an objection that shows the same reasoning leads to the opposite conclusion (that it is not wajib). Hint: identify what the evidence (danger) actually implies and form a syllogism.

The Objection of Division (Taqsīm) in Jadal
Author defines taqsīm, gives two fiqh examples, and states its condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a taqsīm objection in a jadal exchange and verify its validity condition.

Core Idea
Taqsīm attacks ambiguous terms to break the causal link.

This lesson introduces the ninth type of objection in jadal: al-taqsīm (division). It is a powerful technique used against an evidence that contains an ambiguous term. The opponent divides the term into its possible meanings and denies that the cause ('illa) applies to each relevant division, thereby invalidating the proof. We will examine the definition, two fiqh examples, and the condition for a valid taqsīm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Find the term in the proponent's evidence that can bear multiple meanings. In the first example, the term is 'purification' (ṭahārah).
2
Determine the opponent's divisions
List the possible meanings or categories the opponent proposes. For 'purification', the Hanafi divides it into 'cleanliness from impurity' and 'legally-defined ablution'.
3
Check the denial of the cause
For each division, verify whether the opponent denies that the cause (e.g., being an act of worship) applies. In the example, the first division is denied as an act of worship.
4
Verify the condition of acceptability
The division must be based on what the proponent actually said. If the opponent adds a description not mentioned by the proponent, the objection is rejected.
5
Apply to the second example
Repeat the steps for the ownership during option example: identify the term 'sale', the divisions (absolute sale vs. unconditional), and the denial of the cause (ownership) for the first division.

فصل في السؤال التاسع وهو القادح المعروف في الأصول بالتقسيم، وهو قادح عند الجمهور في الدليل، ومنع قوم القدح به. وضابط التقسيم هو أن يحتمل لفظ مُورَدٌ في الدليل معنيين أكثر، بحيث يكون مترددًا بين تلك المعاني والمعترض يمنع وجود علة الحكم في واحد من تلك الاحتمالات. كأن يقول مشترط النية في الوضوء : الطهارة قربة فتشترط فيها النية كغيرها من القرب، فيقول المعترض كالحنفي: الطهارة تنقسم إلى النظافة من الخبث وإلى الأفعال المعروفة المخصوصة التي هي الوضوء شرعًا، والأول ممنوع كونه من القرب التي هي علة وجوب النية . ومن أمثلته أن يُستدل على ثبوت الملك للمشتري في زمن الخيار الملك بوجود سبب وهو البيع، فيقول المعترض : البيع الذي هو سبب الملك ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما مطلق البيع، الصادق بما فيه شرط، والثاني البيع الذي لا شرط فيه، والأول ممنوع كونه علة للملك، والثاني مسلم ولكنه مفقود في محل النزاع . ويشترط لصحة التقسيم شرطان : الأول : أن يكون ما ذكره المستدل منقسمًا إلى ما يمنع وغيره، فلو زاد المعترض وصفًا لم يذكره المستدل لم يُقبل منه .
Section on the ninth question: It is the objection known in the principles [of jurisprudence] as division (taqsim). It is a valid objection according to the majority of scholars in [attacking] an evidence, though some have denied its validity. The definition of division is: that a term used in the evidence can bear two or more meanings, such that it is ambiguous among those meanings, and the opponent denies the existence of the cause of the ruling in one of those probabilities. For example, the one who stipulates intention in ablution says: "Purification is an act of worship, so intention is required in it like other acts of worship." The opponent, like the Hanafi, says: "Purification is divided into cleanliness from impurity and into the well-known specific actions that are the legally-defined ablution. The first is denied to be among the acts of worship that are the cause for the obligation of intention." Another example: One argues for the establishment of ownership for the buyer during the period of option [of rescission] due to the existence of a cause, namely the sale. The opponent says: "The sale which is the cause of ownership is divided into two divisions: one is the absolute sale, which applies to that which includes a condition, and the second is the sale without a condition. The first is denied to be a cause for ownership, and the second is conceded but is absent in the case under dispute." Two conditions are required for the validity of the division: First: that what the proponent mentioned is divisible into what prevents and other [divisions]. So if the opponent adds a description that the proponent did not mention, it is not accepted from him.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage introduces the ninth type of objection in the science of jadal, known as taqsīm (division). The author states that it is accepted by most scholars (jumhūr) as a valid criticism against an evidence, though some have rejected it. The definition is given: it occurs when a term used in the proof is ambiguous between multiple meanings, and the opponent denies that the cause ('illa) of the ruling applies to one of those meanings. Two illustrations from fiqh are provided. The first is from the debate on intention in wudu: the proponent (who requires intention) argues that purification is an act of worship ('ibadah), so it requires intention like other acts of worship. The opponent (a Hanafi) objects by dividing purification into two types: cleanliness from physical impurity (which is not an act of worship) and the specific actions of wudu (the legally-defined purification). The opponent then denies that the first division falls under acts of worship, thus breaking the causal link. The second example concerns ownership during the period of option (khiyar). The proponent argues that sale causes ownership, so the buyer owns the item during the option period. The opponent divides sale into absolute sale (which includes a condition) and unconditional sale. The first type is denied as a cause of ownership, while the second is conceded but not applicable here. Finally, the author states one condition for the validity of this objection: the division must be based on what the proponent actually said. If the opponent adds a description not mentioned by the proponent, the objection is not accepted. (A second condition is implied but not fully stated in this passage; the text mentions 'two conditions' but only gives one explicitly – likely the second was omitted in the OCR or cuts off.)

The Objection by Division: Dismantling Proofs through Exhaustive Division
Defines the objection by division with examples, conditions, and rebuttal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid objection by division against an argument in usul al-fiqh and evaluate its conditions and rebuttal.

Core Idea
Divide the opponent's term, then deny the effective cause in one division to break the proof.

The objection by division is a sophisticated dialectical move that challenges a proof by showing its key term is ambiguous. The objector divides the term into exhaustive possibilities and denies that the effective cause applies to one of them, thereby undermining the proof. This lesson unpacks its definition, examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i debates, and the rules for its valid use.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ambiguous term
Locate the term in the proponent's proof that could have multiple meanings. This is the 'mawrid' that the objector will divide.
E.g., 'tahara' in the intention for wudu argument.
2
Divide the term exhaustively
Split the term into all its possible meanings or types, ensuring the division is exhaustive (hasr). The objector must base the division on the proponent's own wording, not add extra descriptions.
E.g., 'tahara' divides into physical cleanliness and ritual wudu.
3
Deny the effective cause in one division
Assert that the division which would support the proponent's conclusion lacks the effective cause ('illah). This is the 'man'' (denial) that is specific to that division.
E.g., physical cleanliness is not an act of worship requiring intention.
4
Formulate the objection as a syllogism
Structure the objection: Minor premise: 'Your proof is divisible into what proves its falsity and other than that.' Major premise: 'Every such proof does not stand as a proof.' Conclusion: 'Your proof does not stand.'
This makes the logic explicit and ties it to the rules of jadal.
5
Anticipate the proponent's response
The proponent can respond by showing that his term only means one thing, or that it is most apparent in the meaning that supports his case, similar to the response to an inquiry (istifsar).
If the division is not exhaustive, the proponent can also show his term falls outside the objector's classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في السؤال التاسع وهو القادح المعروف في الأصول بالتقسيم، وهو قادح عند الجمهور في الدليل، ومنع قوم القدح به. وضابط التقسيم هو أن يحتمل لفظ مُورَدٌ في الدليل معنيين أكثر، بحيث يكون مترددًا بين تلك المعاني والمعترض يمنع وجود علة الحكم في واحد من تلك الاحتمالات. كأن يقول مشترط النية في الوضوء : الطهارة قربة فتشترط فيها النية كغيرها من القرب، فيقول المعترض كالحنفي: الطهارة تنقسم إلى النظافة من الخبث وإلى الأفعال المعروفة المخصوصة التي هي الوضوء شرعًا، والأول ممنوع كونه من القرب التي هي علة وجوب النية. ومن أمثلته أن يُستدل على ثبوت الملك للمشتري في زمن الخيار بالبيع، فيقول المعترض : البيع الذي هو سبب الملك ينقسم إلى قسمين: أحدهما مطلق البيع، الصادق بما فيه شرط، والثاني البيع الذي لا شرط فيه، والأول ممنوع كونه علة للملك، والثاني مسلم ولكنه مفقود في محل النزاع. ويشترط لصحة التقسيم شرطان : الأول : أن يكون ما ذكره المستدل منقسمًا إلى ما يمنع وغيره، فلو زاد المعترض وصفًا لم يذكره المستدل لم يُقبل منه. والثاني: أن يكون التقسيم حاصرًا لجميع الأقسام، فإن لم يحصرها فللمستدل أن يتبين أن مَوْرِدَه غيرُ ما عينه المعترض بالذكر. ووجه قبول القدح بالتقسيم أن اللفظ إذا احتمل أمرين أحدهما باطل فهو محتمل البطلان فلا تنهض به حجة. ووجه رد القدح به أن احتمال البطلان لا يبطل الدليل. والجواب عن القدح بالتقسيم كالجواب عن الاستفسار المتقدم وهو أن يبين أن لفظه لا يحتمل إلا ذلك المعنى، أو أنه أظهر فيه، وقد أوضحناه [بأمثلته] في الكلام على الاستفسار. وتطبيق هذا القادح على الاعتراض في البحث والمناظرة هو أن التقسيم منع ، ولكنه منع خاص ؛ لأنه منع لا يأتي إلا بعد تقسيم. ووجهه يقول : دليلك هذا منقسم إلى ما يحقق بطلانه وإلى غيره وكل دليل كان كذلك فلا تنهض به حجة، ينتج : دليلك لا تنهض به حجة، فالصغرى تضمنت تقسيما، والكبرى تضمنت منعا بعد التقسيم.
Chapter on the ninth question: It is the objection known in the fundamentals as the objection by division. It is a valid objection according to the majority regarding the proof, but some people denied objecting by it. The definition of division is that a term used in the proof possibly has two or more meanings, such that it is ambiguous among those meanings, and the objector denies the existence of the effective cause (illah) of the ruling in one of those possibilities. For example, one who requires intention in wudu says: Purity (tahara) is an act of worship, so intention is required in it like other acts of worship. The objector, like the Hanafi, says: Purity divides into cleanliness from impurity and the specific known actions which are wudu legally. The first is denied to be among the acts of worship that are the cause of the obligation of intention. Another example: One argues for the establishment of ownership for the buyer during the option period due to the existence of a cause, namely the sale. The objector says: The sale that is the cause of ownership divides into two parts: one is absolute sale, true even with a condition, and the second is a sale without condition. The first is denied to be a cause for ownership, and the second is conceded but it is absent in the disputed case. Two conditions are required for the validity of the division: First, that what the proponent mentioned is divisible into what is denied and what is other; if the objector adds a description that the proponent did not mention, it is not accepted from him. Second, that the division is exhaustive of all parts; if it is not exhaustive, then the proponent can show that his term is other than what the objector specified. The reason for accepting the objection by division is that if a term possibly has two meanings, one of which is false, then it is possibly false, so it does not stand as a proof. The reason for rejecting it is that the possibility of falsity does not invalidate the proof. The answer to the objection by division is like the answer to the previous inquiry, which is to show that his term only has that meaning, or that it is more apparent in it, and we have clarified it with examples in the discussion of inquiry. The application of this objection to the objection in research and debate is that division is a denial, but it is a specific denial because it is a denial that only comes after division. Its formulation says: Your proof is divisible into what proves its falsity and into other than that, and every proof that is like that does not stand as a proof. Thus, the conclusion is: Your proof does not stand as a proof. So the minor premise includes a division, and the major premise includes a denial after the division.
Source pages p. 339

Commentary
This passage defines the 'objection by division' (al-qadih bi al-taqsim), a specific type of dialectical objection used in Islamic legal theory (usul) and debate. It begins by stating that this objection is accepted by the majority of scholars as a valid criticism against a proof, though some reject it. The definition follows: when a term used in a proof has multiple possible meanings, the objector denies that the effective cause (illah) of the ruling applies to one of those meanings. Two examples illustrate this: first, the case of requiring intention in wudu. The proponent argues that tahara is an act of worship, thus requires intention. The Hanafi objector divides tahara into physical cleanliness and the specific ritual actions of wudu, then denies that the first (physical cleanliness) is an act of worship that requires intention. Second, a sale during the option period: the proponent claims ownership is established by the sale. The objector divides sale into absolute (including conditional) and unconditional, denies that the absolute sale is the cause of ownership, and concedes the unconditional one but notes it's absent. Conditions for the division to be valid: (1) the division must be based on what the proponent actually mentioned; adding an extra description is not accepted. (2) The division must be exhaustive; if not, the proponent can claim his term falls outside the objector's classification. The passage then discusses the rationale: if a term can be false, the proof is not decisive. But the counter is that mere possibility doesn't invalidate. The proper response is similar to that for 'inquiry' (istifsar): clarify that the term only means one thing or is most apparent in it. Finally, the application: this objection is a specific type of denial (man'), formulated as a syllogism with a minor premise dividing the proof and a major premise stating that any such divisible proof is not a proof. The language is technical and typical of jadal manuals.

Key Takeaway
The objection by division is a precise dialectical tool that requires an exhaustive division of the proponent's term and a targeted denial of the effective cause in one part, forming a valid jadal move.

The Flaw of Non-Exhaustive Division in Dialectic
Author explains the condition of exhaustive division and how to rebut the flaw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a non-exhaustive division in dialectical argument and apply the correct rebuttal by showing the term is unambiguous or the division is exhaustive.

Core Idea
A division that misses a part destroys a proof — but a skilled debater knows how to rescue it.

In adab al-bahth, the objector often attacks by claiming the proponent's proof is inconclusive because it admits of a false interpretation. This is the flaw of non-exhaustive division. The author explains when this flaw is valid and how the proponent can defend himself using the same strategy as in istifsar (clarification).

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition of exhaustive division
The author states: 'an yakuna al-taqsim hasiran li-jami' al-aqsam' — the division must cover all possible parts. If it does not, the proponent can show his point is different from what the objector specified.
This condition is specific to dialectical contexts, not just logical division.
2
Understand the flaw (qadh bil-taqsim)
The flaw arises when a word admits two meanings, one false. If the division is not exhaustive, the proof is deemed inconclusive. But the author notes this can be rejected because mere possibility of falsehood does not invalidate a proof.
3
Learn the rebuttal
The proponent's response is like that in istifsar: show that the word does not admit other meanings, or that the intended meaning is the most apparent. This blocks the objector's claim of non-exhaustiveness.
The author refers to earlier examples on istifsar for illustration.
4
Recognize the dialectical form
The objection takes the form: 'Your proof is divisible into a part proving its falsehood and other parts; any such proof is inconclusive.' This is a prevention (man') after division. The minor premise contains the division, the major the prevention.
This structure is key to understanding how the flaw is applied in disputation.
5
Apply to a real debate
Practice: In a debate about the attributes of Allah, an objector says your division of attributes into essential and accidental is non-exhaustive. How do you rebut? Use the strategies above.
Ensure you check for exhaustiveness and word clarity.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The second: that the division be exhaustive of all parts. If it is not exhaustive, then the proponent can show that his point is different from what the objector specified by mention. The reason for accepting the flaw of division is that if a word admits two meanings, one of which is false, then it is possible to be false, so it does not serve as a proof. The reason for rejecting the flaw is that the possibility of falsehood does not invalidate the proof. The response to the flaw of division is like the response to the previous clarification, which is that he shows that his word does not admit except that meaning, or that it is more apparent in it, and we have explained it with examples in the discussion on clarification. The application of this flaw to objection in research and disputation is that division is a prevention, but it is a specific prevention, because it is a prevention that only comes after division. Its reasoning says: your proof is divisible into what realizes its falsehood and into other, and every proof that is like that does not serve as a proof, resulting: your proof does not serve as a proof. Commentary
This passage discusses the dialectical flaw (qadih) of non-exhaustive division in disputation. The author states that a division must be exhaustive (hasir) of all possible parts. If the objector's division is not exhaustive, the proponent can show that the point under discussion is not what the objector specified. The flaw is accepted because if a word can mean two things one of which is false, the proof is not conclusive. The Objector's Syllogism: Division and Prevention
Author presents the syllogistic form of the objector's argument on division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the objector's categorical syllogism in the flaw of division and verify its validity.

Core Idea
The objector's argument is a first-figure syllogism using division as the middle term.

The passage presents the objector's argument in a formal syllogistic structure. Understanding this structure helps you recognize how the flaw of division is applied in disputation and how to check its validity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion (natija)
The objector concludes: 'Your proof does not serve as a proof.' This is the statement that the proponent's argument is inconclusive.
2
Find the major premise
The major premise is: 'Every proof that is like that (i.e., divisible into false and other parts) does not serve as a proof.' This is a universal negative statement.
The phrase 'kull dalil kana kadhalik' indicates universality.
3
Find the minor premise
The minor premise is: 'Your proof is divisible into what realizes its falsehood and into other.' This is a singular affirmative statement.
4
Determine the figure and mood
The middle term is 'divisible into false and other parts'. The major term is 'does not serve as a proof'. The minor term is 'your proof'. This is first figure, with mood EAE (Celarent).
The syllogism is valid if premises are true.
5
Evaluate the premises
The proponent can attack the major premise (is it true that every divisible proof is inconclusive?) or the minor premise (is the proof actually divisible in that way?).
This evaluation is the key to the rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The reason for rejecting the flaw is that the possibility of falsehood does not invalidate the proof. The response to the flaw of division is like the response to the previous clarification, which is that he shows that his word does not admit except that meaning, or that it is more apparent in it, and we have explained it with examples in the discussion on clarification. The application of this flaw to objection in research and disputation is that division is a prevention, but it is a specific prevention, because it is a prevention that only comes after division. Its reasoning says: your proof is divisible into what realizes its falsehood and into other, and every proof that is like that does not serve as a proof, resulting: your proof does not serve as a proof. So the minor premise includes a division, and the major premise includes a prevention after division.
Source pages p. 340

Commentary
This passage discusses the dialectical flaw (qadih) of non-exhaustive division in disputation. The author states that a division must be exhaustive (hasir) of all possible parts. If the objector's division is not exhaustive, the proponent can show that the point under discussion is not what the objector specified. The flaw is accepted because if a word can mean two things one of which is false, the proof is not conclusive. But it can be rejected by showing that the word does not admit other meanings or is more apparent in the intended meaning. The application in disputation is that this flaw is a type of prevention (man') that comes specifically after a division. The objector argues: 'Your proof can be divided into a part that proves its falsehood and other parts; any such proof is not conclusive.' So the minor premise is the division, the major is the prevention. The author then links this to the earlier discussion on istifsar (clarification). This is a standard dialectical move in adab al-bahth.

Key Terms
التقسيم
القدح بالتقسيم
المنع

D) The flaw of division

Key Takeaway
The objector's argument is a formally valid first-figure categorical syllogism; rebutting it requires attacking the truth of its premises.

The Tenth Objection: Lack of Effect (Adam al-Ta'thir)
Author classifies the four divisions of the lack-of-effect objection in dialectical argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the different types of 'adam al-ta'thir' in a dialectical argument and determine the validity of the objection.

Core Idea
The 'lack of effect' objection challenges whether a description truly causes the ruling.

The tenth objection in jadal, known as 'adam al-ta'thir', occurs when a proponent mentions a description that is dispensable to the ruling. This lesson explores its divisions, explaining how each challenges the causal link between description and ruling, with examples from fiqh debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the description in question
In the proponent's argument, locate the wasf (description) that is claimed as a cause for the ruling. This is the attribute the proponent uses to analogize.
2
Determine which division applies
Check if the objection targets the description itself (tardi), the original case (alternative cause), or the ruling (benefit or no benefit). The text gives distinct examples for each.
3
Assess the validity of the objection
Apply the rules of jadal: if the description is tardi, the objection is valid; if the objector presents an alternative cause, check whether he denies multiple causes; if the description has no benefit in the ruling, the objection is valid; if it has a necessary benefit, the objection may be overruled.
4
Apply to examples from the text
Use the Hanafi prayer example for the first division, the sale of absent thing for the second, and the apostates and istijmar examples for the third division to practice classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في السؤال العاشر وهو القادح المعروف في الأصول بعدم التأثير، أي عدم تأثير الوصف في الحكم. وضابطه أن يُذكر في الدليل ما يُستغنى عنه، وهو أربعة أقسام، وبعضهم يجعله ثلاثة بإسقاط الرابع الآتي: الأول من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الوصف، وضابطه أن يكون الوصف طرديا لا مناسبة فيه أصلاً كقول الحنفي في صلاة: صلاةٌ لا تُقصر، فلا يقدم أذانها على الوقت كالمغرب، فعدم القصر طردي في تقديم الأذان؛ لأن الصلوات التي تقصر لا يقدم أذانها على الوقت. وحاصل هذا القسم منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديًا، وهو أحد أقسام المنع المتقدمة. الثاني من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الأصل، وضابطه إبداء المعترض علةً لحكم الأصل غير علة المستدل. بشرط كون المعترض يرى منع تعدد العلة لحكم واحد، أما إن كان يرى جواز ذلك يصح قدحه بهذا القسم؛ لجواز صحة كلتا العلتين. ومثاله أن يقال في بيع الغائب: بيع غير مرئي فلا كالطير في الهواء، فيقول المعترض: لا أثر لكونه غير مرئي في الأصل؛ فإن العجز عن التسليم كاف في عدم الصحة، وعدمها واقع مع الرؤية، وهذا القسم راجع إلى المعارضة في الأصل كما تقدم إيضاحه. الثالث من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الحكم، وهو ثلاثة أضرب: الأول منها: ألا يكون لذكره فائدة أصلاً، ومثلوا له بقول الحنفي في المرتدين مشركون أتلفوا مالا بدار الحرب فلا ضمان عليهم كالحربي، ودار الحرب عندهم لا أثر لذكرها في الأصل ولا في الفرع؛ لأن من أوجب الضمان ومن نفاه لم يفرق أحد منهم بين دار الحرب وغيرها. وهذا راجع إلى القسم الأول، وهو منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديا، فالمعترض يطالب المستدل بتأثير كون الإتلاف في دار الحرب، والذي عليه المحققون فساد العلة بذلك، وذهب بعضهم إلى صحة التمسك به، ولا يخفى ما فيه. الضرب الثاني: أن يكون لذكر الوصف فائدة ضرورية، كقول العدد معتبر في الاستجمار بالأحجار عبادة متعلقة بالأحجار لم تتقدمها معصية، فاعتُبر فيها العدد قياسًا على رمي الجمار. فقوله (لم تتقدمها معصية) عديم التأثير في الأصل والفرع، لكنه مضطر إلى ذكره ليحترز به عن الرجم؛ لأنه عبادة متعلقة بالأحجار ولم يُعتبر فيها العدد، لكنها تقدمتها معصية هي الزنى بعد الإحصان.
Section on the tenth question, which is the well-known objection in the principles regarding lack of effect, i.e., the lack of effect of the description on the ruling. Its rule is that something dispensable is mentioned in the proof, and it has four divisions, though some make it three by dropping the fourth that follows: The first of its divisions: it is called lack of effect in the description, and its rule is that the description is incidental (tardi) with no suitability at all, like the Hanafi's statement about prayer: 'A prayer that is not shortened, so its call to prayer is not advanced before the time, like Maghrib.' Thus, non-shortening is incidental in advancing the call because prayers that are shortened do not have their call advanced before the time. The gist of this division is preventing the description from being a cause because it is incidental, and it is one of the previously mentioned divisions of prevention. The second of its divisions: it is called lack of effect in the original case, and its rule is that the objector presents a cause for the ruling of the original other than the proponent's cause, on the condition that the objector holds to preventing multiple causes for a single ruling; but if he holds it permissible, his objection by this division is valid because both causes could be valid. An example is when it is said about selling an absent thing: 'A sale not seen, so it is like a bird in the air.' Then the objector says: 'There is no effect for it being unseen in the original case, because inability to deliver suffices for unsoundness, and unsoundness occurs even with sight.' This division goes back to objection in the original case, as previously explained. The third of its divisions: it is called lack of effect in the ruling, and it has three sub-types: The first of them: that its mention has no benefit at all, and they gave as an example the Hanafi's statement about apostates: 'They are polytheists who destroyed property in Dar al-Harb, so there is no liability on them, like the Harbi.' Dar al-Harb according to them has no effect in its mention in the original or the branch because those who impose liability and those who negate it, none of them differentiated between Dar al-Harb and others. This goes back to the first division, i.e., preventing the description from being a cause because it is incidental, so the objector demands that the proponent provide the effect of the destruction being in Dar al-Harb. The verdict of the investigators is the corruption of the cause by that, though some went to the soundness of clinging to it, and its flaw is not hidden. The second sub-type: that the mention of the description has a necessary benefit, like the statement: 'Number is considered in istijmar with stones, an act of worship related to stones not preceded by sin, so number is considered in it by analogy to throwing the jamarat.' His statement 'not preceded by sin' has no effect in the original or the branch, but he is compelled to mention it to guard against stoning (rajm), because it is an act of worship related to stones in which number is not considered, but it was preceded by sin, namely adultery after ihsan.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the tenth objection in dialectic (jadal), known as 'lack of effect' (adam al-ta'thir). The author defines it as mentioning something in the proof that is dispensable, i.e., it has no real impact on the ruling. He then enumerates its divisions: The first division is 'lack of effect in the description' (adam al-ta'thir fi al-wasf). Here, the description is purely incidental (tardi) with no suitability to the ruling. The example given is the Hanafi argument about a prayer that is not shortened (like the dawn prayer? Actually the example is about a prayer that is not shortened, so its adhan is not given before the time, by analogy to Maghrib. The description 'non-shortening' is incidental because the prayers that are shortened also do not have adhan before time; thus it is not a true cause. The second division is 'lack of effect in the original case' (adam al-ta'thir fi al-asl). Here, the objector presents an alternative cause for the ruling in the original case, different from the proponent's cause. This is valid only if the objector believes that a single ruling cannot have multiple causes (i.e., one cause suffices). The example is the sale of an absent thing (bay' al-gha'ib): the proponent says it is invalid because it is unseen, like a bird in the air. The objector counters that the real cause is inability to deliver, which exists even when the object is seen, so the 'unseen' description has no effect. The third division is 'lack of effect in the ruling' (adam al-ta'thir fi al-hukm), which has three sub-types. The first sub-type: the mention of the description has no benefit at all. Example: the Hanafi argument about apostates destroying property in Dar al-Harb has no liability, like the Harbi. The description 'in Dar al-Harb' is irrelevant because both those who impose liability and those who negate it do not distinguish between Dar al-Harb and other territories. This reduces to the first division (incidental description). The second sub-type: the mention of the description has a necessary benefit, but it does not actually influence the ruling. Example: in the issue of istijmar (cleansing with stones), the proponent argues that number is required because it is an act of worship related to stones not preceded by sin, analogous to throwing jamarat. The description 'not preceded by sin' is necessary to distinguish it from stoning (rajm) which is also an act of worship related to stones but does not require number. However, this description has no effect in the original or the branch; it is merely a distinguishing factor, not a cause.

Key Terms
عدم التأثير
طردي
منع كون الوصف علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The description is universally applicable.
D) The description is opposed by another description.

Key Takeaway
The 'lack of effect' objection in jadal has three main divisions: in the description (tardi), in the original (alternative cause), and in the ruling (two sub-types: no benefit or necessary benefit).

First Type of Non-Effect: No Benefit from Descriptor
Author defines the first type where mentioning the descriptor has no benefit at all, with example from Hanafi law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first type of the defect 'non-effect in the ruling' (ʿadam al-taʾthīr fī al-ḥukm) and evaluate whether a descriptor in a legal analogy is entirely without benefit.

Core Idea
A descriptor with no benefit eliminates the cause and invalidates the analogy.

In dialectical reasoning (jadal/munāẓara), identifying defects in the opponent's analogy is crucial. One key defect is 'non-effect in the ruling,' where the mentioned descriptor (waṣf) fails to influence the ruling. The first type occurs when the descriptor has no benefit at all in either the original or derivative case. The Hanafi example of apostates in the Abode of War illustrates this: the location is irrelevant because both sides of the ruling do not differentiate based on it. Recognizing this defect allows you to challenge weak analogies and demand a truly effective cause.

Guided Path
1
Identify the descriptor
Find the term that is claimed to be the cause (ʿilla) in the analogy. In the Hanafi example, the descriptor is 'destruction in the Abode of War'.
2
Check effect in the original case
Does the descriptor affect the ruling in the original (aṣl)? The Hanafi say no, because the ruling (no liability) applies to all apostates regardless of location.
3
Check effect in the derivative case
Does the descriptor affect the ruling in the branch (farʿ)? Again, no, because the analogy is to the Harbi, and location is not a factor.
4
Determine if benefit exists
If the descriptor has no benefit in either the original or the branch, it is the first type of non-effect. The objector can demand that the arguer demonstrate an effect; if unable, the analogy is invalid.
This reduces to the earlier principle that a co-extensive (ṭardī) descriptor is not a valid cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إيضاحه . الثالث أقسامه : من هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الحكم، القسم وهو ثلاثة أضرب :
الأول منها : ألا يكون لذكره فائدة أصلاً، ومثلوا له بقول الحنفي في المرتدين مشركون أتلفوا مالا بدار الحرب فلا ضمان عليهم كالحربي، ودار الحرب عندهم لا أثر لذكرها في الأصل ولا في الفرع ؛ لأن من أوجب الضمان ومن نفاه لم يفرق أحد منهم بين دار الحرب وغيرها .
وهذا راجع إلى القسم الأول، وهو منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديا، فالمعترض يطالب المستدل بتأثير كون الإتلاف في دار الحرب، والذي عليه المحققون فساد العلة بذلك، وذهب بعضهم إلى صحة التمسك به، ولا يخفى ما فيه .
Its clarification. The third: its divisions: that which is called non-effect in the ruling, the section is three types: The first of them: that there is no benefit at all in mentioning it. They gave an example by the saying of the Hanafi regarding apostates: 'Polytheists who destroyed property in the Abode of War, so there is no liability upon them, like the Harbi.' But the Abode of War according to them has no effect in mentioning it in the original or in the branch, because whoever imposes liability and whoever negates it, none of them distinguished between the Abode of War and other than it. And this goes back to the first section, which is prevention of the descriptor being the cause because it is co-extensive (tardī). So the objector demands from the arguer the effect of the destruction being in the Abode of War. And the position of the verifiers is the corruption of the cause by that, and some held the validity of adhering to it, and what is in it is not hidden.

Commentary
This passage defines the first type of the defect called 'non-effect in the ruling'. The author begins by stating that this section has three types. The first type is when mentioning the descriptor provides no benefit at all—neither in the original case (al-aṣl) nor in the derivative case (al-farʿ). The example is from Hanafi jurisprudence: apostates who destroyed property in the Abode of War have no liability, analogized to the Harbi. The descriptor 'in the Abode of War' has no effect because both sides that impose or negate liability do not differentiate based on location. The author then explains that this type reduces to the first section which is 'preventing the descriptor from being the cause because it is co-extensive' (ṭardī). The objector demands that the arguer show the effect of the descriptor. The verifiers hold that this invalidates the cause (fasād al-ʿilla), while some claim it is still valid. The author notes that the weakness of the latter position is apparent.

Second Type of Non-Effect: Descriptor with Necessary Benefit
Author defines the second type where the descriptor has a necessary benefit to exclude a counterexample.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a descriptor with necessary benefit in an analogical argument and explain why it is excluded from causal effect.

Core Idea
A descriptor with necessary benefit prevents a counterexample without being a cause.

In dialectic, not every descriptor mentioned in an analogy is a cause. Some are included solely to block a potential counterexample. This lesson dissects the second type of non-effect, where the descriptor has a necessary benefit but no actual causal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy and the descriptor
Find the original case (asl), the derivative case (far'), and the descriptor being questioned. In the example, the analogy is that number is considered in istijmar by analogy to ramy al-jimar, and the descriptor is 'not preceded by sin'.
2
Check the descriptor's effect on the ruling
Determine whether the descriptor has any effect on the ruling in the original or the derivative. The passage states it has no effect in either ('عديم التأثير في الأصل والفرع').
3
Identify the necessary benefit
Ask: what counterexample would refute the analogy if this descriptor were omitted? The benefit is exclusionary: it prevents the opponent from objecting using rajm, which is similar but lacks the descriptor, thus number is not considered there.
4
Confirm the descriptor is not a cause
Verify that the descriptor is not a cause ('illa). It is only mentioned to block a specific counterexample. The passage calls this a 'necessary benefit' (fāʾida ḍarūriyya).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثاني: أن يكون لذكر الوصف فائدة ضرورية، كقول العدد معتبر في الاستجمار بالأحجار عبادة متعلقة بالأحجار لم تتقدمها معصية، فاعتُبر فيها العدد قياسًا على رمي الجمار . فقوله (لم تتقدمها معصية) عديم التأثير في الأصل والفرع، لكنه مضطر إلى ذكره ليحترز به عن الرجم ؛ لأنه عبادة متعلقة بالأحجار ولم يُعتبر فيها العدد، لكنها تقدمتها معصية هي الزنى بعد الإحصان .
The second type: that mentioning the descriptor has a necessary benefit, like the saying: 'Number is considered in istijmar with stones, it is a worship related to stones not preceded by sin, so number was considered in it by analogy to ramy al-jimar.' So his saying 'not preceded by sin' has no effect in the original and the branch, but he is compelled to mention it to exclude stoning (rajm), because it is a worship related to stones but number was not considered in it, yet it was preceded by sin which is adultery after ihṣān.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage explains the second type of non-effect. Here, the descriptor does not affect the ruling in either the original or the derivative case, but it is mentioned for a necessary benefit: to avoid a counterexample. The example is from the ruling that the number of stones used in istijmar (cleansing with stones) is considered, analogized to ramy al-jimar (stoning the pillars in Hajj). Both are acts of worship involving stones and not preceded by sin. However, the counterexample is rajm (stoning for adultery) which is also a worship involving stones but number is not considered because it is preceded by sin. So the descriptor 'not preceded by sin' is added to exclude rajm, even though it has no causal effect in itself.

Key Takeaway
The second type of non-effect shows that a descriptor may be necessary to mention even though it has no causal effect, simply to block a counterexample.

Third Type of Non-Effect: Non-Necessary Benefit
Author defines the third type where the descriptor has a non-necessary benefit that only strengthens the analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a descriptor provides a non-necessary benefit that strengthens an analogy in a jadal argument.

Core Idea
A descriptor may be non-essential yet still strengthen the analogy.

In jadal, when constructing an analogy, not every descriptor needs to be essential for the ruling. Some descriptors are mentioned merely to enhance the similarity between the branch and the original, making the analogy more convincing. This lesson explores the third type of non-effect, where the descriptor has a non-necessary benefit that only strengthens the analogy without being essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the analogy and its components
Find the original (asl) and the branch (far') in the example. The original is Zuhr prayer, the branch is Friday prayer. The ruling is that neither requires the supreme imam's permission.
2
Locate the descriptor in question
The descriptor is 'obligatory' (mafrūḍa) applied to Friday prayer. Ask: is this descriptor necessary for the analogy?
3
Determine if the descriptor is essential
The author states that omitting 'obligatory' would not harm the analogy. So it is not essential for the ruling.
Contrast with essential descriptors that exclude counterexamples.
4
Assess the benefit it provides
The benefit is to strengthen the similarity between Friday and Zuhr: both are obligatory prayers. The descriptor 'obligatory' makes the branch more similar to the original.
The text says: 'for the obligatory is more similar to the obligatory than to other than it'.
5
Consider the scholarly disagreement
Some scholars prevented rejecting such a descriptor when it is mentioned for a benefit. The author says this view 'has a direction' (i.e., is justified). Reflect on why this might be debated in jadal.
This disagreement touches on how strictly we evaluate the relevance of each descriptor in an analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث: أن يكون لذكر الوصف فائدة غير ضرورية، كأن يقول : الجمعة صلاة مفروضة فلا تفتقر في إقامتها إلى إذن الإمام الأعظم كالظهر. فقوله (مفروضة) لو حذف لما ضر ، لكنه ذكر لفائدة تقريب الفرع من الأصل بتقوية الشبه بينهما ؛ إذ الفرض أشبه بالفرض منه لغيره . ومنع قوم رد ما ذُكر لفائدة، وله اتجاه .
The third type: that mentioning the descriptor has a non-necessary benefit, like one saying: 'Friday prayer is an obligatory prayer, so it does not need the permission of the supreme imam for its establishment, like Zuhr prayer.' So his saying 'obligatory' if omitted it would not harm, but it is mentioned for the benefit of bringing the branch closer to the original by strengthening the similarity between them, for the obligatory is more similar to the obligatory than to other than it. And some prevented rejecting what is mentioned for a benefit, and it has a direction.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
The third type is when the descriptor provides a benefit that is not necessary. The example is the analogy between Friday prayer and Zuhr prayer: Friday is an obligatory prayer, so it does not require the supreme imam's permission, just as Zuhr does not. The descriptor 'obligatory' (mafrūḍa) is not essential for the ruling, but it strengthens the similarity between the branch (Friday) and the original (Zuhr). The author notes that some scholars prevented rejecting such a descriptor when it is mentioned for a benefit, and this view has some justification.

Key Terms
فائدة غير ضرورية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is the cause for not needing the imam's permission.
B) It excludes a counterexample like supererogatory prayers.
C) It strengthens the analogy by making Friday more similar to Zuhr.
D) It has no benefit and could be omitted.

Key Takeaway
In jadal, a descriptor that is not essential to the ruling can still have a valuable role: it strengthens the analogy by enhancing the perceived similarity between the branch and the original.

Understanding the Fourth Division: Non-Effect in the Branch
Author defines the fourth division called 'non-effect in the branch' with example from marriage without guardian.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases of 'non-effect in the branch' in dialectical objections and explain why it reduces to non-effect in the original.

Core Idea
A descriptor with no effect on the ruling in the branch collapses into a simpler defect.

In dialectic (jadal), arguments are tested by objections. One such objection is when a descriptor used as a cause in a branch case actually has no impact on the ruling. This lesson explores the fourth division of defects called 'non-effect in the branch' and shows how it ultimately reduces to a more basic category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original claim and the objection
The original claim: a woman marrying herself without guardian's permission is invalid, analogized to marrying a non-compatible man. The objector says the descriptor 'non-compatible' has no effect because the ruling is the same whether the man is compatible or not.
The branch (farʿ) is the derivative case being analogized.
2
Recognize the defect: no effect in the branch
The objector argues that the descriptor (non-compatible) does not affect the ruling in the branch. The ruling (invalidity) remains regardless of compatibility. Thus, the descriptor is ineffective.
This is precisely the definition of 'non-effect in the branch'.
3
Identify the underlying objection: muʿāraḍa with another descriptor
The author explains that this defect reduces to an objection (muʿāraḍa) where the objector presents an alternative descriptor – 'marriage' itself – which is more suitable as the cause. The dispute is about the marriage contract, not compatibility.
Some scholars omitted this division because it is already covered under 'preventing effect in the original'.
4
Confirm the reduction: back to non-effect in the original
By presenting 'marriage' as the descriptor, the objector is essentially showing that the original descriptor (non-compatible) has no effect even in the original case. Thus, the fourth division is not a separate defect but a variant of an earlier one.
This reduction is important for economy of classification in dialectic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الرابع من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الفرع ، كأن يقال في تزويج المرأةِ نفسها : بغير إذن وليها فلا يصح، كما لو زوجت من غير كفء . فيقول المعترض : كونه غير كفء لا أثر له، لأن النزاع واقع في تزويجها من كفء ومن غير كفء، وحكمهما سواء فلا أثر له . ومرجعه إلى المعارضة بوصف آخر وهو (تزويج) فقط، ولهذا حذف بعضُهم هذا القسم الرابع ؛ لأنه راجع إلى منع التأثير في الأصل.
The fourth division of its divisions: it is called non-effect in the branch (al-farʿ), like if it is said regarding a woman marrying herself: 'Without the permission of her guardian, it is not valid, just as if she married a non-compatible man.' So the objector says: 'His being non-compatible has no effect, because the dispute occurs about her marriage to a compatible and to a non-compatible man, and their ruling is the same, so it has no effect.' And its reference is to the objection (muʿāraḍa) with another descriptor which is 'marriage' only, and because of this some omitted this fourth division, because it goes back to preventing effect in the original.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth division of the defect called 'non-effect in the branch'. The example is from the case of a woman marrying herself without a guardian's permission. The arguer claims it is invalid, analogizing to marrying a non-compatible man (she needs a guardian). The objector responds that the descriptor 'non-compatible' has no effect because the ruling is the same whether the man is compatible or not; the dispute is about the marriage contract itself, not about compatibility. Thus, the descriptor has no effect in the branch. The author notes that this division essentially reduces to an objection (muʿāraḍa) by presenting another descriptor (i.e., the mere act of marriage) and some scholars omitted it because it is already covered under preventing effect in the original.

Key Takeaway
The fourth division, non-effect in the branch, is not a separate defect but a special case of the general objection that a descriptor has no effect, reducing to non-effect in the original.

The Two Foundations of the Non-Effect Defect
Author concludes that all divisions of the non-effect defect revert to two principles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two foundations of the non-effect defect and recognize their application in a jadal exchange.

Core Idea
All defects of 'non-effect' in dialectic reduce to just two principles: prevention and objection.

In this passage, the author distills the previously discussed divisions of the non-effect defect down to two fundamental types. This simplification is crucial for mastering the art of jadal, as it provides a clear framework for analyzing objections. Let's work through how these two principles operate in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two principles
Locate the two things all divisions revert to: prevention (manʿ) because the descriptor is co-extensive (ṭardī), and objection (muʿāraḍa) by presenting another suitable descriptor. Note the Arabic terms: منع and معارضة.
2
Understand the basis of prevention
Prevention is specifically tied to the descriptor being ṭardī (co-extensive), meaning it does not effectively cause the ruling. This is a common objection in uṣūl al-fiqh.
Co-extensiveness means the descriptor applies to all instances of the ruling but does not explain why.
3
Understand the basis of objection
Objection involves offering an alternative descriptor that is also suitable as a cause (ʿilla). This challenges the original causal claim by providing a competing explanation.
The alternative must be ṣāliḥ li-l-taʿlīl (suitable for explanation).
4
Apply to a dialectical example
Consider a debate about whether wine is prohibited because of intoxication. A prevention objection would argue that intoxication is not effective because it is co-extensive (every intoxicant is prohibited, but not all prohibitions are intoxicants). An objection would present another descriptor, e.g., 'it is a liquid that intoxicates' as the real cause.
This shows how the two principles operate in a concrete jadal exchange.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد عرفت مما ذكرنا أن أقسام القادح المسمى بعدم التأثير راجعة إلى شيئين لا ثالث لهما: أحدهما منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديا . والثاني : المعارضة بإبداء وصف آخر صالح للتعليل، وتطبيق المنع والمعارضة في هذا القادح على مامر في البحث والمناظرة واضح . واعلم أن القدح بهذا القادح المسمى في الأصول بعدم التأثير
And you have known from what we mentioned that the divisions of the defect called non-effect revert to two things, no third: one of them is preventing the descriptor from being the cause because it is co-extensive (tardī). The second: the objection by presenting another descriptor suitable for explanation. And the application of prevention and objection in this defect to what has passed in research and debate is clear. And know that the criticism by this defect called in the principles (uṣūl) non-effect...
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage summarizes the previous discussion. The author states that all divisions (aqsām) of the defect called 'non-effect' (ʿadam al-taʾthīr) go back to only two things: (1) preventing the descriptor from being a cause because it is merely co-extensive (ṭardī), i.e., it is not effective; (2) the objection (muʿāraḍa) by presenting another descriptor that is suitable for being the cause (taʿlīl). The application of these two in debate is clear. The author then begins to note that this defect is known in uṣūl al-fiqh as 'non-effect', but the text is cut off.

معارضة
قادح

Key Takeaway
All non-effect defects in dialectic ultimately rest on either preventing the descriptor as cause due to co-extensiveness or objecting by presenting an alternative descriptor.

The Third and Fourth Types of Lack of Effect
Author explains third and fourth types of 'adam al-ta'thir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third and fourth types of 'adam al-ta'thir in a jadal passage and reduce any instance to either prevention or opposition.

Core Idea
Every 'adam al-ta'thir objection reduces to preventing the cause or opposing it with a better one.

In this lesson, we explore two specific forms of the objection of lack of effect: when an attribute provides only a non-essential benefit, and when the attribute has no effect on the branch ruling. The author illustrates each with concrete examples from Islamic law and then shows that all forms reduce to just two fundamental challenges: prevention of causation and opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of 'adam al-ta'thir
Read the examples: Friday prayer (third type: attribute 'obligatory' is non-essential) and woman's marriage (fourth type: attribute 'non-kuf' has no effect on ruling). Determine which type fits the scenario.
The third type has a benefit but omission doesn't break analogy; the fourth type shows the ruling is unchanged with or without the attribute.
2
Extract the underlying logical structure
For the third type, the attribute strengthens resemblance but is not necessary for the analogy. For the fourth, the attribute is irrelevant because the ruling is identical whether present or absent.
The author emphasizes that the fourth type reduces to opposition (mu'arada).
3
Reduce to core objections
The author states all divisions reduce to two things: preventing the attribute from being the cause (man' kawn al-wasf 'illa) if it is accidental, or opposing it with another attribute more suitable for causation.
Check the example: in the fourth type, the objector opposes by presenting 'marriage' as the real cause.
4
Apply the reduction to a new example
Given a new analogy, determine whether the objection is actually a prevention or an opposition. This helps in constructing a proper response.
This step trains you to see through the form to the essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث: أن يكون لذكر الوصف فائدة غير ضرورية، كأن يقول : الجمعة صلاة مفروضة فلا تفتقر في إقامتها إلى إذن الإمام الأعظم كالظهر. فقوله (مفروضة) لو حذف لما ضر ، لكنه ذكر لفائدة تقريب الفرع من الأصل بتقوية الشبه بينهما ؛ إذ الفرض أشبه بالفرض منه لغيره . ومنع قوم رد ما ذُكر لفائدة، وله اتجاه . القسم الرابع من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الفرع ، كأن يقال في تزويج المرأةِ نفسها : بغير إذن وليها فلا يصح، كما لو زوجت من غير كفء . فيقول المعترض : كونه غير كفء لا أثر له، لأن النزاع واقع في تزويجها من كفء ومن غير كفء، وحكمهما سواء فلا أثر له . ومرجعه إلى المعارضة بوصف آخر وهو (تزويج) فقط، ولهذا حذف بعضُهم هذا القسم الرابع ؛ لأنه راجع إلى منع التأثير في الأصل. وقد عرفت مما ذكرنا أن أقسام القادح المسمى بعدم التأثير راجعة إلى شيئين لا ثالث لهما: أحدهما منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديا . والثاني : المعارضة بإبداء وصف آخر صالح للتعليل، وتطبيق المنع والمعارضة في هذا القادح على مامر في البحث والمناظرة واضح . واعلم أن القدح بهذا القادح المسمى في الأصول بعدم التأثير يُشترط في القدح به أن يكون القياس قياس علة، فلا يُقدح به في قياس الشبه ، ولا في الطرد على القول باعتباره، ويُشترط فيه أيضًا أن تكون العلة مستنبطة مختلفًا فيها، فلا يُقدح به في علة منصوصة، ولا مستنبطة مجمع عليها . واعلم أن القادح المسمى في الأصول بالمطالبة والقادح المسمى بالتركيب تركنا ذكرهما لأنهما داخلان في أقسام المنع التي أوضحناها، فذكرهما تكرار محض مع ذكر أقسام المنع؛ لأن المطالبة هي بعينها منع كون الوصف (١) ، وقد أوضحناهما وبينا أنهما من أقسام المنع . (١) سياق الكلام يوحي بوجود سقط هنا لعل هذا معناه : [موجودًا في الأصل، والتركيب هو بعينه منع كون الوصف علة للحكم]. وانظر ما سبق ص ٣١٣، ٣١٤ .
The third type: that the mention of the attribute has a non-essential benefit, such as saying: Friday is an obligatory prayer, so it does not need the permission of the supreme imam for its performance, like Zuhr. His saying (obligatory) if omitted would not harm, but it was mentioned for the benefit of bringing the branch closer to the root by strengthening the resemblance between them; indeed, the obligatory is more similar to the obligatory than to other than it. And some people prevented the refutation of what was mentioned for a benefit, and it has a direction. The fourth division of its divisions: it is called lack of effect in the branch, such as saying concerning a woman marrying herself: without the permission of her guardian it is not valid, just as if she married someone not of her status (ghayr kuf'). The objector says: his being not of status has no effect, because the dispute is concerning her marrying someone of status and someone not of status, and their ruling is the same, so it has no effect. And its reference is to opposition with another attribute, which is (marriage) only, and for this some omitted this fourth division; because it returns to preventing the effect in the root. And you have known from what we mentioned that the divisions of the objection called lack of effect reduce to two things, no third: one of them: preventing the attribute from being the cause because it is accidental (tardi). The second: opposition by presenting another attribute suitable for causation (ta'lil). And the application of prevention and opposition in this objection to what has passed in research and debate is clear. And know that objecting with this objection called in usul (principles) lack of effect requires that the analogy be an analogy of cause (qiyas 'illa), so it is not used to object in an analogy of similarity (qiyas shabah), nor in accidental (tard) according to those who consider it. And it is also required that the cause be derived and disputed; so it is not used to object in a textual cause (mansusa) nor in a derived cause that is unanimously agreed upon. And know that the objection called in usul al-mutalaba (demand) and the objection called al-tarkib (composition) we have left mentioning them because they are included in the divisions of prevention which we have clarified; so mentioning them is pure repetition with mentioning the divisions of prevention; because al-mutalaba is identical to preventing the attribute (1), and we clarified them and showed that they are from the divisions of prevention. (1) The context suggests a lacuna here; perhaps its meaning is: [present in the original, and al-tarkib is identical to preventing the attribute from being the cause for the ruling]. And see what preceded pp. 313, 314.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the objection known as 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) used in dialectical argumentation. The author first describes the third type: when an attribute is mentioned for a non-essential benefit. The example is the argument that Friday prayer is obligatory (mafruda) and thus does not require the imam's permission, like Zuhr. The attribute 'obligatory' is not essential to the analogy; omitting it would not harm the reasoning, but it is mentioned to strengthen the similarity between the branch (Friday) and the root (Zuhr) because both are obligatory. Some scholars reject such objections, seeing the benefit as legitimate. The fourth type is the actual 'lack of effect' in the branch: e.g., a woman marrying herself without a guardian's permission is invalid, just as if she married a non-kuf' (unsuitable match). The objector points out that the attribute 'non-kuf'' has no effect because the ruling is the same whether the husband is kuf' or not; thus the attribute is irrelevant. This type reduces to opposition with another attribute (marriage itself), which is why some omit it as redundant. The author then summarizes that all divisions of 'adam al-ta'thir go back to two things: (1) preventing the attribute from being a cause because it is accidental (tardi), and (2) opposing it by presenting a more suitable cause. Next, conditions: this objection applies only to qiyas 'illa (analogy based on cause), not to qiyas shabah (similarity) nor to tard (coincidental correlation), and the cause must be derived (mustanbata) and disputed (mukhtalaf fiha), not textual (mansusa) or unanimously agreed upon. Finally, the objections of mutalaba (demand) and tarkib (composition) are not separately discussed because they are already covered under the divisions of prevention (man').

Key Terms
عدم التأثير
الضرب الثالث
القسم الرابع
قياس علة
منع كون الوصف علة
المعارضة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) is essential to the analogy and its omission invalidates it.
B) provides a non-essential benefit such as strengthening resemblance.
C) has no effect on the ruling in the branch.
D) is a textual cause (mansusa).

Key Takeaway
The objection of lack of effect comes in multiple forms, but all ultimately reduce to either preventing the attribute from being the cause or opposing it with a more suitable cause.
