Conditions and Scope of the Lack of Effect Objection
Author states conditions for using 'adam al-ta'thir and subsumes mutalaba and tarkib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine when the objection of lack of effect is applicable and distinguish it from other objections like mutalaba and tarkib.

Core Idea
The objection of lack of effect only works in analogies based on a derived, disputed cause, not in similarity or unanimous causes.

The author specifies two critical conditions for using 'adam al-ta'thir: the analogy must be qiyas 'illa (causal analogy), not qiyas shabah (similarity) or tard (coincidental), and the cause must be derived (mustanbata) and disputed (mukhtalaf fiha). He also clarifies that other well-known objections like mutalaba and tarkib are already included within the prevention-type objections, so they need not be treated separately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of analogy
Is the analogy causal (qiyas 'illa) or based on similarity? The objection of lack of effect only applies to causal analogies. If it is qiyas shabah or tard, the objection is invalid.
The text explicitly says: 'يُشترط في القدح به أن يكون القياس قياس علة'.
2
Check the cause's status
Is the cause textual (mansusa) or unanimously agreed upon? If so, the objection cannot be used. It must be derived and disputed.
The cause must be mustanbata (derived) and mukhtalaf fiha (disputed).
3
Recognize subsumed objections
The author says mutalaba and tarkib are not separate objections; they are forms of preventing the attribute (man' kawn al-wasf 'illa). Understand that demanding evidence for causation (mutalaba) or pointing out composition (tarkib) are essentially saying the attribute is not the true cause.
This avoids redundancy in debate.
4
Apply the conditions to a new case
Given a scenario with a textual cause, explain why 'adam al-ta'thir cannot be used. This step solidifies the conditions.
For example, the prohibition of riba based on a textual 'illa cannot be objected to with lack of effect.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الضرب الثالث: أن يكون لذكر الوصف فائدة غير ضرورية، كأن يقول : الجمعة صلاة مفروضة فلا تفتقر في إقامتها إلى إذن الإمام الأعظم كالظهر. فقوله (مفروضة) لو حذف لما ضر ، لكنه ذكر لفائدة تقريب الفرع من الأصل بتقوية الشبه بينهما ؛ إذ الفرض أشبه بالفرض منه لغيره . ومنع قوم رد ما ذُكر لفائدة، وله اتجاه . القسم الرابع من أقسامه: هو المسمى بعدم التأثير في الفرع ، كأن يقال في تزويج المرأةِ نفسها : بغير إذن وليها فلا يصح، كما لو زوجت من غير كفء . فيقول المعترض : كونه غير كفء لا أثر له، لأن النزاع واقع في تزويجها من كفء ومن غير كفء، وحكمهما سواء فلا أثر له . ومرجعه إلى المعارضة بوصف آخر وهو (تزويج) فقط، ولهذا حذف بعضُهم هذا القسم الرابع ؛ لأنه راجع إلى منع التأثير في الأصل. وقد عرفت مما ذكرنا أن أقسام القادح المسمى بعدم التأثير راجعة إلى شيئين لا ثالث لهما: أحدهما منع كون الوصف علة لكونه طرديا . والثاني : المعارضة بإبداء وصف آخر صالح للتعليل، وتطبيق المنع والمعارضة في هذا القادح على مامر في البحث والمناظرة واضح . واعلم أن القدح بهذا القادح المسمى في الأصول بعدم التأثير يُشترط في القدح به أن يكون القياس قياس علة، فلا يُقدح به في قياس الشبه ، ولا في الطرد على القول باعتباره، ويُشترط فيه أيضًا أن تكون العلة مستنبطة مختلفًا فيها، فلا يُقدح به في علة منصوصة، ولا مستنبطة مجمع عليها . واعلم أن القادح المسمى في الأصول بالمطالبة والقادح المسمى بالتركيب تركنا ذكرهما لأنهما داخلان في أقسام المنع التي أوضحناها، فذكرهما تكرار محض مع ذكر أقسام المنع؛ لأن المطالبة هي بعينها منع كون الوصف (١) ، وقد أوضحناهما وبينا أنهما من أقسام المنع . (١) سياق الكلام يوحي بوجود سقط هنا لعل هذا معناه : [موجودًا في الأصل، والتركيب هو بعينه منع كون الوصف علة للحكم]. وانظر ما سبق ص ٣١٣، ٣١٤ .
The third type: that the mention of the attribute has a non-essential benefit, such as saying: Friday is an obligatory prayer, so it does not need the permission of the supreme imam for its performance, like Zuhr. His saying (obligatory) if omitted would not harm, but it was mentioned for the benefit of bringing the branch closer to the root by strengthening the resemblance between them; indeed, the obligatory is more similar to the obligatory than to other than it. And some people prevented the refutation of what was mentioned for a benefit, and it has a direction. The fourth division of its divisions: it is called lack of effect in the branch, such as saying concerning a woman marrying herself: without the permission of her guardian it is not valid, just as if she married someone not of her status (ghayr kuf'). The objector says: his being not of status has no effect, because the dispute is concerning her marrying someone of status and someone not of status, and their ruling is the same, so it has no effect. And its reference is to opposition with another attribute, which is (marriage) only, and for this some omitted this fourth division; because it returns to preventing the effect in the root. And you have known from what we mentioned that the divisions of the objection called lack of effect reduce to two things, no third: one of them: preventing the attribute from being the cause because it is accidental (tardi). The second: opposition by presenting another attribute suitable for causation (ta'lil). And the application of prevention and opposition in this objection to what has passed in research and debate is clear. And know that objecting with this objection called in usul (principles) lack of effect requires that the analogy be an analogy of cause (qiyas 'illa), so it is not used to object in an analogy of similarity (qiyas shabah), nor in accidental (tard) according to those who consider it. And it is also required that the cause be derived and disputed; so it is not used to object in a textual cause (mansusa) nor in a derived cause that is unanimously agreed upon. And know that the objection called in usul al-mutalaba (demand) and the objection called al-tarkib (composition) we have left mentioning them because they are included in the divisions of prevention which we have clarified; so mentioning them is pure repetition with mentioning the divisions of prevention; because al-mutalaba is identical to preventing the attribute (1), and we clarified them and showed that they are from the divisions of prevention. (1) The context suggests a lacuna here; perhaps its meaning is: [present in the original, and al-tarkib is identical to preventing the attribute from being the cause for the ruling]. And see what preceded pp. 313, 314.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the objection known as 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) used in dialectical argumentation. The author first describes the third type: when an attribute is mentioned for a non-essential benefit. The example is the argument that Friday prayer is obligatory (mafruda) and thus does not require the imam's permission, like Zuhr. The attribute 'obligatory' is not essential to the analogy; omitting it would not harm the reasoning, but it is mentioned to strengthen the similarity between the branch (Friday) and the root (Zuhr) because both are obligatory. Some scholars reject such objections, seeing the benefit as legitimate. The fourth type is the actual 'lack of effect' in the branch: e.g., a woman marrying herself without a guardian's permission is invalid, just as if she married a non-kuf' (unsuitable match). The objector points out that the attribute 'non-kuf'' has no effect because the ruling is the same whether the husband is kuf' or not; thus the attribute is irrelevant. This type reduces to opposition with another attribute (marriage itself), which is why some omit it as redundant. The author then summarizes that all divisions of 'adam al-ta'thir go back to two things: (1) preventing the attribute from being a cause because it is accidental (tardi), and (2) opposing it by presenting a more suitable cause. Next, conditions: this objection applies only to qiyas 'illa (analogy based on cause), not to qiyas shabah (similarity) nor to tard (coincidental correlation), and the cause must be derived (mustanbata) and disputed (mukhtalaf fiha), not textual (mansusa) or unanimously agreed upon. Finally, the objections of mutalaba (demand) and tarkib (composition) are not separately discussed because they are already covered under the divisions of prevention (man').

Conditions for Valid Objection in Qiyas 'Illa
Author sets conditions for valid objection in analogical argument and subsumes two objections under man'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether an objection (qadih) is valid against an analogical argument (qiyas 'illa) by checking the type of qiyas and the nature of the cause.

Core Idea
Only objections against a deduced and disputed cause in qiyas 'illa are valid.

In dialectical debates on Islamic legal analogy, not all objections are permissible. This passage sets the boundaries: you can only object (qadih) when the argument is a qiyas 'illa with a cause that is deduced and disputed. It also clarifies that two specific objections—mutalaba and tarkib—are already included under the broader category of man' (prevention). Understanding these conditions prevents redundancy and strengthens your debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Type of Qiyas
Check whether the argument is a qiyas 'illa (analogy of the effective cause). If it is qiyas shabah (resemblance) or tard (co-occurrence), the objection is invalid.
2
Examine the Cause ('Illa)
The cause must be deduced (mustanbata) and disputed (mukhtalif fiha). If the cause is textual (mansusa) or unanimously agreed (mujma' 'alayha), no objection is allowed.
3
Recognize Subsumed Objections
The objections 'mutalaba' (demand for proof of attribute) and 'tarkib' (composition) are not separate; they are forms of man' (prevention). Understand that they are already covered under the divisions of man'.
This avoids redundancy in debate.
4
Apply to a Debate Scenario
Given a debate where a claimant uses qiyas 'illa with a cause, decide if an opponent's objection is valid based on the conditions above.
Example: Objection to qiyas shabah is invalid.

يُشترط في القدح به أن يكون القياس قياس علة، فلا يُقدح به في قياس الشبه، ولا في الطرد على القول باعتباره، ويُشترط فيه أيضًا أن تكون العلة مستنبطة مختلفًا فيها، فلا يُقدح به في علة منصوصة، ولا مستنبطة مجمع عليها. واعلم أن القادح المسمى في الأصول بالمطالبة والقادح المسمى بالتركيب تركنا ذكرهما لأنهما داخلان في أقسام المنع التي أوضحناها، فذكرهما تكرار محض مع ذكر أقسام المنع؛ لأن المطالبة هي بعينها منع كون الوصف [موجودًا في الأصل، والتركيب هو بعينه منع كون الوصف علة للحكم].
It is required for objecting by it that the syllogism be a syllogism of cause, so one does not object by it in a syllogism of resemblance, nor in [syllogism of] co-occurrence according to the opinion that it is considered. And it is also required that the cause be deduced and disputed, so one does not object by it in a textual cause, nor in a deduced cause that is unanimously agreed upon. And know that the objection called in Usul [al-Fiqh] 'al-mutalaba' and the objection called 'al-tarkib' we have left mentioning them because they are included in the divisions of prevention (al-man') which we have clarified, so mentioning them is pure repetition with the mention of the divisions of prevention; because al-mutalaba is exactly preventing the attribute from being [present in the original], and al-tarkib is exactly preventing the attribute from being the cause of the ruling.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage defines the conditions under which an objection (qadih) may be raised against an analogical argument (qiyas) in dialectical debate. The author specifies that the objection is valid only if the syllogism in question is a 'qiyas 'illa' (syllogism of the effective cause). It cannot be used against a 'qiyas shabah' (syllogism of resemblance) or 'al-tard' (syllogism of co-occurrence, which some consider a valid argument). Additionally, the cause ('illa) must be deduced (mustanbata) from the ruling and must be a matter of dispute (mukhtalif fiha). If the cause is textual (mansusa, explicitly stated in revelation) or deduced but unanimously agreed upon (mujma' 'alayha), then the objection is not permitted. The author then notes that they deliberately omitted two specific types of objections—al-mutalaba (demand for the cause) and al-tarkib (composition)—because these are already covered under the general category of prevention (man'). Al-mutalaba is essentially a prevention of the attribute's existence in the original case, while al-tarkib is a prevention of the attribute as the cause of the ruling. Thus, discussing them separately would be redundant. This clarification helps the debater focus on valid grounds for objection without repetition.

Key Takeaway

Al-Qawl bi al-Mujab: Conceding the Argument, Not the Conclusion
Author defines al-qawl bi al-mujab and begins enumerating its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of al-qawl bi al-mujab and distinguish its first type from other dialectical objections.

Core Idea
Conceding an argument does not mean conceding the dispute.

In dialectical debate, an opponent can accept the proponent's reasoning yet still deny the conclusion. This powerful tool, called al-qawl bi al-mujab, is a hallmark of Islamic jurisprudence (usul al-fiqh) and debate (adab al-bahth). The passage defines it, illustrates it with a Quranic example, and begins analyzing its four types. We focus on the definition and the first type.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
The passage gives the dabit (essence): 'تسليم المعترض دليل الخصم مع بقاء النزاع'. Understand that the dispute remains because the conceded argument does not address the actual point of contention (mahall al-niza').
2
Analyze the Quranic example
Abdullah ibn Ubayy argued that the mightier (hypocrites) would expel the humbler (Muslims). Allah conceded the general principle but showed that it does not apply because hypocrites are actually the humbler. The dispute remains; Ibn Ubayy's argument is neutralized.
3
Understand the four-type framework
The author states that al-qawl bi al-mujab occurs in four faces. We focus on the first face: defect in the side of negation (taraf al-nafi).
4
Examine the first type's structure
The proponent intends to refute something imagined to be the basis of his own doctrine. Refuting that imagined basis does not refute his actual doctrine. Most instances of al-qawl bi al-mujab are of this type.
5
Consider the fiqh example (incomplete)
The passage begins an example about retaliation (qisas) with a heavy object: disparity in means (e.g., sword vs. stone) does not prevent qisas. The example breaks off, but the principle is that the opponent concedes the rule but denies it applies to the specific case.
The example is incomplete due to page cut-off.

Chapter on the eleventh question: it is the objection called in Usul [al-Fiqh] 'al-qawl bi al-mujab'. Its essence is that the opponent concedes the proponent's argument while the dispute remains, by making the argument which he conceded not be the subject of dispute, so from its soundness and concession the soundness of the proponent's doctrine does not follow. Like His - Exalted - statement: {They say: If we return to Medina, the mightier will surely expel the humbler from it} [Al-Munafiqun]; so Ibn Ubayy in this noble verse argued that he would expel the Messenger of Allah and his companions from Medina because the mightier is able to expel the humbler, and Allah - Glorious and Exalted - conceded to him this argument, explaining that it does not benefit him in the subject of dispute because he is the humbler who is capable of being expelled, as in His - Exalted - statement: {But honor belongs to Allah and His Messenger and the believers, but the hypocrites...} the verse. And know that al-qawl bi al-mujab according to the usuliyun occurs on four faces: The first face: that it comes for a defect in the side of negation, and that is because the proponent intends by the argument to refute something that is imagined to be the basis of his doctrine, so from its refutation the refutation of his doctrine does not follow, and most of al-qawl bi al-mujab is of this type. Such as if it is said concerning the obligation of retaliation with a heavy object: disparity in the means, like instruments of killing and others, does not prevent retaliation, like the end sought, such as killing or amputation or others: disparity therein does not prevent retaliation, so disparity in instruments like being with a sword or spear or others, and disparity in killing like being by beheading or cutting a limb, and disparity in cutting like being by cutting the joint from one side or two sides, or
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage introduces the eleventh type of objection in dialectical debate, known in Usul al-Fiqh as 'al-qawl bi al-mujab' (the affirmative statement). The author defines it: the opponent (mu'tarid) concedes the proponent's (khasm) argument but maintains that the dispute remains unresolved. The key is that the conceded argument does not address the actual point of contention (mahall al-niza'). Therefore, even if the argument is sound and the opponent concedes it, the proponent's doctrine is not necessarily established. A clear Qur'anic example is given from Surah Al-Munafiqun: 'Abdullah ibn Ubayy argued that the mightier (the hypocrites) would expel the humbler (the Muslims) from Medina, reasoning that the mightier can expel the humbler. Allah conceded the general principle but pointed out that the hypocrites themselves were the humbler (since true honor belongs to Allah, His Messenger, and the believers), so the argument does not apply to them. The author then notes that according to the usul scholars, al-qawl bi al-mujab occurs in four types. The first type (al-wajh al-awwal) arises from a defect in the negative side (nafi). In this situation, the proponent uses his argument to refute something that is mistakenly thought to be the basis of his own doctrine. However, refuting that imagined basis does not refute his actual doctrine. Most instances of al-qawl bi al-mujab belong to this type. An example is given (though incomplete) regarding the obligation of retaliation (qisas) for killing with a heavy object (muthqal). The argument runs: disparity in the means of killing (e.g., using a sword vs. a heavy object) does not prevent the obligation of qisas, just as disparity in the result (killing vs. amputation) does not prevent it. The example continues, but the text breaks off at the page end.

محلة النزاع
طرف النفي

C) Rejecting the opponent's argument entirely
D) Proposing a new argument to replace the opponent's

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi al-mujab forces the debater to distinguish between conceding a general principle and acknowledging its applicability to the disputed case.

Al-Qawl bi al-Mujib: The Objection of Conceding the Proof
Defines al-qawl bi al-mujib and its first two types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first two types of al-qawl bi al-mujib in legal arguments and explain why the opponent's concession does not refute the dispute.

Core Idea
Conceding the proof does not concede the conclusion if the proof is irrelevant.

One of the most powerful yet subtle dialectical moves is al-qawl bi al-mujib: the objector accepts the arguer's proof but denies it addresses the actual point of contention. This lesson unpacks the definition and distinguishes its first two types using a Quranic example and a classic fiqh dispute over qisas with heavy objects. Mastering this concept will sharpen your ability to detect irrelevant refutations in any debate.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the opening definition: al-qawl bi al-mujib occurs when the objector (المعترض) concedes the proof (دليل) of the opponent (المستدل) but insists that the proof does not pertain to the actual point of dispute (محل النزاع). The dispute remains unresolved despite the conceded proof.
The key is that the proof itself is accepted, but its relevance is denied.
2
Analyze the Quranic example
Consider the story of Ibn Ubayy in Surah al-Munafiqun. Ibn Ubayy argued that the mightier expels the weaker, so the Muslims would be expelled. God conceded the logical premise (the mightier expels the weaker) but clarified that Ibn Ubayy himself is the weaker, so the proof does not support his claim.
The objected conceded the proof but showed it works against the objector, not for him.
3
Identify the first type: defect in the negative
The first type occurs when the arguer's proof aims to invalidate something the opponent mistakenly holds as the basis of his doctrine. The objector concedes the invalidation but argues that the true basis is something else entirely. Example: In the qisas dispute, the Hanafi concedes that variation in means does not prevent retaliation, but insists that the real basis for denying qisas is the absence of intent to kill.
The arguer's negation is correct but irrelevant—it attacks a straw man.
4
Identify the second type: concerning affirmation
The second type involves the objector conceding that the proof's implication (e.g., no conflict with qisas) is true, but denying that the proof establishes the disputed ruling itself. In the same qisas example, the Hanafi concedes that killing with a heavy object does not conflict with qisas, but argues that the proof does not entail the necessity of qisas.
Here the concession is about an affirmative implication, not a negation.
5
Compare the two types
Both types share the structure: the objector concedes the proof while denying its relevance. The difference lies in whether the conceded proof targets a negation (first type) or an affirmation (second type). In both cases, the arguer fails to refute the opponent's actual position.
Always ask: does the objection target a negation or an affirmation of a claimed basis or implication?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في السؤال الحادي عشر وهو القادح المسمى في الأصول بالقول بالموجب، وضابطه تسليم المعترض دليل الخصم مع بقاء النزاع، وذلك بجعل الدليل الذي سلّمه ليس هو محل النزاع، فلا يلزم من صحته وتسليمه صحةً مذهب المستدل به كقوله - تعالى -: ﴿ يَقُولُونَ لَين رَجَعْنَا إِلَى الْمَدِينَةِ لَيُخْرِجَ الْأَعَزُّ مِنْهَا الْأَذَلَّ ﴾ [المنافقون ]، فابن أبي في هذه الآية الكريمة استدل على أنه يُخرج الرسول الله وأصحابه من المدينة بأن الأعزّ قادر على إخراج الأذل، والله - جل وعلا ـ سلّم له هذا الدليل مبينًا أنه لا ينفعه في محل النزاع ؛ لأنه هو الأذل المقدور على إخراجه، وذلك في قوله - تعالى -: ﴿ وَلِلَّهِ الْعِزَّةُ وَلِرَسُولِهِ، وَلِلْمُؤْمِنِينَ وَلَكِنَّ الْمُنَفِقِينَ ) الآية . واعلم أن القول بالموجب عند الأصوليين يقع على أربعة أوجه : الوجه الأول: أن يرد لخلل في طرف النفي، وذلك أن المستدل من الدليل إبطال أمر يتوهم منه أنه مبنى مذهبه، فلا يلزم من إبطاله إبطال مذهبه، وأكثر القول بالموجب من هذا النوع . كأن يقال في وجوب القصاص بالمثقل : التفاوت في الوسيلة من آلات القتل وغيره لا يمنع القصاص، كالمتوسل إليه من قتل أو قطع أو غيرهما : لا يمنع التفاوتُ فيه القصاص، فتفاوت الآلات ككونه بسيف أو رمح أو غيرهما، وتفاوتُ القتل ككونه بحر عني أو قطع عضو، وتفاوت القطع ككونه بحزّ المفصل من جهة واحدة أو من جهتين، أو بغير ذلك فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : سلّمنا أن التفاوت في الوسيلة لا يمنع القصاص، ولكن لا يلزم من إبطال مانع انتفاء جميع الموانع ووجود جميع الشروط بعد قيام المقتضي. وثبوت القصاص متوقف على ذلك، فقول المستدل (لا يمنع القصاص) نفي، ولأجل ما وقع فيه من الخلل ورد القول بالموجب، فكأن الحنفي يقول للمستدل : ما توهمت أنه مبنى مذهبي في القصاص في القتل بالمثقل ليس هو مبناه، فلا يلزم من إبطاله إبطال مذهبي بل مبنى مذهبي شيء آخر لم تتعرض له في اعتراضك ومعلوم أن موجب منع الحنفي القصاص في القتل بالمثقل عدم تحقق العلة التي هي قصد القتل، فهو عنده من الخطأ شبه العمد ؛ إذ لا يلزم من قصده ضربه بالمثقل قصده إزهاق روحه [ عنده ] (١) . الوجه الثاني : أن يقع على ثبوت وضابطه أن يستنتج المستدل من الدليل ما يُتوهم منه أنه محل النزاع أو مُلازمه ولا يكون كذلك. كأن يقال في القصاص في القتل بالمثقل : قتل بما يقتل غالبًا لا ينافي القصاص، فيجب فيه القصاص قياسًا على الإحراق بالنار . فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : سلّمنا عدم المنافاة بين القتل بمثقل وبين ثبوت القصاص، ولكن لم قلت إن القتل بمثقل يستلزم القصاص) وذلك هو محل النزاع، ولم يستلزمه دليلك ، ، وهو العلة التي (١) في الأصل عنه، وما أثبته: عنه، وما أثبته هو الموافق للسياق .
Chapter on the eleventh question, which is the objection called in the principles (usul) 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' (the statement with the affirmative). Its principle is that the objector concedes the proof of the opponent while the dispute remains, by making the conceded proof not be the subject of dispute, so its validity and concession do not necessitate the validity of the doctrine of the one who argued with it. For example, His statement, Exalted: 'They say: If we return to Medina, the mightier will surely drive out the weaker' [Surah al-Munafiqun]. So Ibn Ubayy in this noble verse argued that he would expel the Messenger of Allah and his companions from Medina by [the premise that] the mightier is able to expel the weaker, and God, Glorious and Exalted, conceded to him this proof, clarifying that it does not benefit him in the subject of dispute because he is the weaker who is able to be expelled, and that is in His statement, Exalted: 'And to Allah belongs might, and to His Messenger, and to the believers, but the hypocrites ...' the verse. Know that 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' according to the usuliyyun occurs in four types: The first type: that it occurs due to a defect in the negative side, and that is that the arguer intends by the proof to invalidate a matter that is imagined to be the basis of his doctrine, so its invalidation does not necessitate the invalidation of his doctrine, and most of 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' is of this type. As it is said concerning the obligation of retaliation (qisas) with a heavy object (al-muthqal): Variation in the means from instruments of killing and others does not prevent retaliation, like the intended act of killing, cutting off, or others: variation in it does not prevent retaliation. So variation in instruments like it being with a sword, spear, or others, and variation in killing like it being by cutting off a limb or cutting a joint from one side or two sides, or otherwise. The objector, like the Hanafi, says: We concede that variation in the means does not prevent retaliation, but invalidating one obstacle does not necessitate the absence of all obstacles and the presence of all conditions after the existence of the efficient cause. The establishment of retaliation depends on that. So the arguer's statement 'does not prevent retaliation' is a negation, and due to the defect that occurred in it, 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' is applied. So it is as if the Hanafi says to the arguer: What you imagined to be the basis of my doctrine concerning retaliation in killing with a heavy object is not its basis, so its invalidation does not necessitate the invalidation of my doctrine; rather, the basis of my doctrine is something else that you did not address in your objection. And it is known that the reason for the Hanafi's prohibition of retaliation in killing with a heavy object is the non-realization of the effective cause ('illa), which is the intention to kill, for in his view it is a mistake akin to premeditated murder (shibh al-'amd), since intending to strike him with a heavy object does not entail intending to take his life, in his view. The second type: that it occurs concerning affirmation, and its principle is that the arguer deduces from the proof what is imagined to be the subject of dispute or its necessary concomitant, but it is not so. As it is said concerning retaliation in killing with a heavy object: Killing with what usually kills does not conflict with retaliation, so it obligates retaliation by analogy with burning by fire. The objector, like the Hanafi, says: We concede the absence of conflict between killing with a heavy object and the establishment of retaliation, but why did you say that killing with a heavy object entails retaliation? That is the subject of dispute, and your proof does not entail it, and it is the effective cause ('illa) which... (1) In the original: 'عن', and what we have established is 'عنه', and what we have established is in agreement with the context.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is from a work on dialectical method (adab al-bahth) and explains al-qawl bi al-mujib, a key objection in Islamic legal reasoning. The author first defines it: the objector (المعترض) concedes the opponent's proof (dawah) but insists that the proof does not address the actual point of dispute. The example from Surah al-Munafiqun is classic: Ibn Ubayy argued that the mightier would expel the weaker from Medina, implying the Muslims would be expelled. God conceded the argument but showed that Ibn Ubayy himself was the weaker party, so the proof did not establish his claim. The author then states that usuliyyun divide al-qawl bi al-mujib into four types, but only the first two are detailed here. The first type occurs when there is a defect in the negative side of the argument. The arguer (المستدل) uses a proof to negate something that the opponent mistakenly thinks is the basis of his doctrine. The example deals with retaliation for killing with a heavy object. The Hanafi (opponent) objects: we accept that variation in means does not prevent retaliation, but removing one obstacle does not prove all conditions for retaliation are met. The arguer's negation is flawed, so the objection applies. The second type concerns affirmation: the arguer's proof yields something that is imagined to be the dispute or its equivalent but is not. In the same example, the arguer claims killing with a heavy object is like burning (which entails retaliation), but the Hanafi concedes the proof's implication that there is no conflict, but denies that it proves the ruling itself. Both types are illustrated with fiqh (Islamic jurisprudence) examples, specifically from the law of retaliation (qisas), demonstrating that this dialectical tool is rooted in the Islamic sciences.

Key Terms
القول بالموجب
المعترض
المستدل
مبنى المذهب
العلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first type of al-qawl bi al-mujib, what is the defect in the arguer's proof?
A) The proof is invalid because the premises are false.
B) The proof negates something that is not actually the basis of the opponent's doctrine.
C) The proof is conceded but its conclusion is not related to the dispute.
D) The proof is rejected outright by the opponent.

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi al-mujib is a dialectical move where the objector concedes the proof but denies its relevance, exposing it as a straw man or irrelevant implication—a tool to prevent pseudo-refutations.

First Face of al-Qawl bi'l-Mujab: Conceding a Negation
Author explains the first face where the objector concedes a negated premise but denies its implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the first face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab and analyze how an objector can concede a negated premise without conceding the conclusion.

Core Idea
In al-qawl bi'l-mujab, conceding a negation does not concede the entire argument.

In this lesson, you will encounter the first face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab, a key technique in Islamic dialectics. The passage shows how a Hanafi jurist responds to an argument about retaliation (qisas) by conceding the proponent's negative statement but pointing out that its nullification does not prove his case. This illustrates a subtle yet powerful move in disputation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim and premise
The proponent (mustadil) argues that retaliation applies because discrepancy in means does not prevent it. Locate the proponent's statement: 'لا يمنع القصاص' (does not prevent retaliation). This is a negation.
2
Identify the objector's concession
The objector (mu'tarid) says: 'سلّمنا أن التفاوت في الوسيلة لا يمنع القصاص' (We concede that discrepancy in means does not prevent retaliation). He accepts the negated premise.
This is the 'mujab' (positive) part: he concedes the statement as it stands.
3
Understand the objector's denial of implication
Despite conceding the premise, the objector denies that it proves the case. He argues: 'لا يلزم من إبطال مانع انتفاء جميع الموانع' (nullifying one obstacle does not entail absence of all obstacles). The proponent's negation is insufficient.
The objector uses al-qawl bi'l-mujab to accept the form but reject the force of the argument.
4
Recognize the basis of the objector's own position
The Hanafi clarifies that his school's real basis is the absence of intent to kill (قصد القتل), not the means. Therefore, the proponent's attack misses the mark.
This shows that al-qawl bi'l-mujab often leads to clarifying the actual point of contention.
5
Summarize how this illustrates the first face
The first face applies when the proponent's premise is a negation. The objector concedes the negation but denies its relevance or sufficiency for the conclusion.
This is distinct from other faces where the premise is affirmative.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

All of that in other than that. The objector, like the Hanafi, says: We concede that the discrepancy in the means does not prevent retaliation, but the nullification of one obstacle does not necessitate the absence of all obstacles and the existence of all conditions after the existence of the effective cause. The establishment of retaliation depends on that. So the proponent's statement 'does not prevent retaliation' is a negation, and because of the flaw in it, the 'conceding the positive' response is made. It is as if the Hanafi says to the proponent: What you thought is the basis of my school concerning retaliation for killing with a heavy object is not its basis; so nullifying it does not nullify my school. Rather, the basis of my school is something else that you did not address in your objection. It is known that the reason for the Hanafi's prevention of retaliation in killing with a heavy object is the non-fulfilment of the cause, which is the intent to kill. Commentary
This passage explains the first face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab (conceding the positive). The author describes a scenario where the proponent's argument includes a negative statement (a negation), and the objector (here a Hanafi jurist) responds by conceding that statement but pointing out that it does not entail the desired conclusion. The example concerns retaliation (qisas) for killing with a heavy object (al-muthqal). The Hanafi opponent grants that the discrepancy in means does not prevent retaliation, but argues that removing this one obstacle does not guarantee that all conditions for retaliation are met. Therefore, the proponent's negation is insufficient to prove his case. The Hanafi clarifies that his school's position is based on a different cause: the absence of intent to kill, which makes the act resemble quasi-intentional homicide (shibh al-amd). This illustrates how al-qawl bi'l-mujab functions when the proponent's statement is a negation.

Second Face of al-Qawl bi'l-Mujab: Affirmation
Author explains the second face where the objector concedes an affirmative statement but denies it yields the claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab in a jadal exchange and explain how the objector concedes an affirmative premise but denies the entailment to the conclusion.

Core Idea
Conceding a premise does not concede the conclusion.

The second face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab occurs when the objector concedes an affirmative proposition (thubut) from the proponent's argument, but argues that this proposition does not actually lead to the proponent's conclusion. In this lesson, we will dissect the rule and the example from the text to see how this dialectical move works in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's claim
Find the statement that the proponent asserts as an affirmation (thubut). In the example, it is 'retaliation is obligatory for killing with a heavy object'.
This is the conclusion the proponent wants to establish.
2
Locate the objector's concession
The Hanafi objector says: 'We concede the non-preclusion between killing with a heavy object and the establishment of retaliation.' This is the premise he accepts.
He does not concede the conclusion.
3
Identify the dispute over entailment
The objector denies that the premise entails the conclusion. He asks: 'Why do you say that killing with a heavy object entails retaliation?' That is the point of dispute.
The proponent's cause (ʿilla) is not sufficient to prove the conclusion.
4
Understand the objector's move
The objector uses al-qawl bi'l-mujab by accepting the proponent's statement (the non-preclusion) but rejecting that it yields the claim (retaliation). This is the second face: the proponent's statement is an affirmation but not a necessary concomitant of the evidence.
The objector wins this exchange because the entailment is not established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني : أن يقع على ثبوت وضابطه أن يستنتج المستدل من الدليل ما يُتوهم منه أنه محل النزاع أو مُلازمه ولا يكون كذلك. كأن يقال في القصاص في القتل بالمثقل : قتل بما يقتل غالبًا لا ينافي القصاص، فيجب فيه القصاص قياسًا على الإحراق بالنار . فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : سلّمنا عدم المنافاة بين القتل بمثقل وبين ثبوت القصاص، ولكن لم قلت إن القتل بمثقل يستلزم القصاص) وذلك هو محل النزاع، ولم يستلزمه دليلك ، وهو العلة التي هي قوله (قتل بما يقتل غالبًا لا ينافي القصاص)، فقوله (يجب فيه القصاص ثبوت، ولأجل ما ورد فيه من الخلل عنده وقع عليه القول بالموجب المذكور.
The second face: that it occurs on an affirmation, and its rule is that the proponent infers from the evidence what is thought to be the point of dispute or its necessary concomitant, but it is not so. For example, concerning retaliation for killing with a heavy object, it is said: A killing with something that usually kills does not preclude retaliation, so retaliation is obligatory for it, by analogy to burning with fire. The objector, like the Hanafi, says: We concede the non-preclusion between killing with a heavy object and the establishment of retaliation, but why do you say that killing with a heavy object entails retaliation? That is the point of dispute, and your evidence does not entail it. The evidence is the cause, which is his statement: 'Killing with something that usually kills does not preclude retaliation.' So his statement 'retaliation is obligatory for it' is an affirmation, and because of the flaw in it in his view, the aforementioned al-qawl bi'l-mujab occurred upon it.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage presents the second face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab, which occurs when the proponent's statement is an affirmation (thubut) that is thought to entail the conclusion but does not. The author defines its rule: the proponent infers something from the evidence that seems to be the point of dispute or its necessary concomitant, but in reality is not. The example again involves retaliation for killing with a heavy object. The proponent argues: killing with an instrument that usually kills (like a heavy object) does not preclude retaliation, so retaliation is obligatory by analogy to burning with fire. The Hanafi objector concedes the non-preclusion (i.e., that it does not preclude retaliation) but denies that this entails that retaliation is actually obligatory. He points out that the proponent's cause (ʿilla) is not sufficient to prove the conclusion; the proponent's statement 'retaliation is obligatory' is an affirmation that is not entailed by the evidence. Thus, the objector uses al-qawl bi'l-mujab by accepting the premise but rejecting the conclusion.

Key Takeaway
The second face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab shows that conceding an affirmative premise does not obligate accepting the conclusion if the entailment is not established.

The Third Face: Restricting a General Term to an Agreed Case
Author explains the third face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab and its weakness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the third face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab and explain why it is considered the weakest type.

Core Idea
Restricting a general term to an agreed case fails when the proponent's intent is clear.

In dialectical exchange (munāẓara), one of the objector's refutation techniques is al-qawl bi'l-mujab, where he accepts the proponent's statement but turns it against him. The third face occurs when the proponent's expression is general and includes a case both parties agree on. The objector then restricts the statement to that agreed case alone, leaving the disputed part untouched. However, this move is weak because the proponent can clarify his intended meaning, as shown in the example of zakat on horses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structural pattern
Notice that the proponent's wording (lafẓ) is general and covers multiple cases — some agreed upon (muttafaq ʿalayhā), some disputed. The objector seizes on the generality.
2
Understand the objector's move
The objector applies (yaḥmilu) the statement exclusively to the agreed case, thereby avoiding its application to the disputed case. This is the core of the third face.
3
Examine the example from zakat
The Hanafi claims: 'Horses are racing animals, so zakat is obligatory on them like camels.' The objector responds: 'I accept this only if the horses are for trade.' The agreed case is 'horses for trade' which both sides likely accept requires zakat.
4
Assess why it is the weakest
The proponent clarifies: 'I meant zakat on the horses themselves (their value), not just when they are for trade.' Once the proponent states his intended meaning (ʿanaytu), the objector's restriction becomes invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثالث : أن يقع لشمول لفظ المستدل صورة متفقا عليها، فيحمله المعترض على تلك الصورة، ويبقى النزاع فيما عداها . كقول الحنفي في وجوب زكاة الخيل حيوان يسابق عليه فتجب فيه الزكاة كالإبل . فيقول المعترض : أقول به إذا كانت الخيل للتجارة، وهذا أضعف أنواعه ؛ لأن المستدل يقول : عنيت وجوب الزكاة في رقابها .
The third face: that it occurs due to the proponent's wording covering an agreed-upon case, so the objector applies it to that case only, and the dispute remains on other cases. For example, the Hanafi's statement concerning the obligation of zakat on horses: 'An animal on which racing is done, so zakat is obligatory on it, like camels.' The objector says: 'I say it if the horses are for trade.' And this is the weakest of its types because the proponent says: 'I meant the obligation of zakat on the horses themselves.'
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage explains the third face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab, which arises when the proponent's expression is general and includes a scenario that is agreed upon by both parties. The objector then interprets the statement narrowly, applying it only to that agreed case, thereby avoiding the broader implication that would support the proponent's claim. The example is from zakat on horses: the Hanafi proponent argues that horses are animals used for racing, so zakat is obligatory on them, analogizing to camels. The objector responds by accepting the argument only if the horses are trade goods, which is a case generally agreed to require zakat. The author notes this is the weakest type because the proponent explicitly means zakat on the horses themselves (i.e., the animals' value), not just when they are for trade. This shows how al-qawl bi'l-mujab can be used to restrict a general statement to a mutually accepted subset.

Key Terms
شمول لفظ
صورة متفقا عليها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the third face considered the weakest type of al-qawl bi'l-mujab?
A) The objector fails to produce a counterexample
B) The proponent clearly states his intended meaning, making the restriction invalid
C) The agreed case is irrelevant to the dispute
D) The analogy to camels is weak

Key Takeaway
The third face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab is the objector's attempt to restrict a general statement to an agreed case, but it fails if the proponent clarifies his intended meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Task 1: In a debate about zakat on vegetables, a proponent says: 'Every edible food measured by volume is subject to zakat, like wheat.' The objector responds: 'I accept that only for staple crops.' Is this a valid third-face objection? Explain why or why not using the criteria from this lesson.

Fourth Face: Exploiting Omitted Premises in Dialectic
Author explains the fourth face where the proponent omits a premise to avoid objection, and the objector exploits the gap.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fourth face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab and formulate the objector's response when the proponent omits the minor premise.

Core Idea
Silence on a premise is a strategic risk: the objector can accept the stated premise yet deny the conclusion.

In dialectical debates, the proponent sometimes omits a premise—usually the minor premise—to avoid direct denial by the objector. However, this tactic allows the objector to exploit the gap by conceding the stated premise but rejecting the conclusion, as the missing premise is not established. This fourth face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab requires the objector to recognize the omission and respond appropriately.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proponent's stated premise
The proponent states only the major premise (e.g., 'Every act of worship requires intention') but omits the minor premise. Underline the premise that is explicitly given.
2
Locate the omitted premise
What statement is deliberately left out? In the example, it is 'Wudu and ghusl are acts of worship.' This is the minor premise.
3
Understand the objector's response
The objector accepts the stated major premise but denies that the conclusion follows, because the minor premise is missing. He says, 'I concede the major, but it does not entail the desired conclusion.'
4
Evaluate the proponent's motive
Why did the proponent omit? Out of fear that the opponent would deny the minor premise if stated. This is a strategic choice, but it gives the objector an opening.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الرابع : أن يقع لأجل سكوت المستدل عن مقدمة غير مشهورة؛ مخافة منع الخصم لها لو صرح بها . كقول مشترط النية في الوضوء والغُسل : كل ماهو قُربة تُشترط فيه النية كالصلاة ويسكت عن المقدمة الصغرى التي هي : الوضوء والغسل قُربة . فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : أُسلّم أن كل ماهو قربة تُشترط فيها النية ، ولا يلزم من ذلك اشتراطها في الوضوء والغُسل . فلو صرح المستدل بالصغرى فمنعها المعترض خرج عن القول بالموجب. وبعضهم يقول في هذا المثال السكوت عن مقدمة مشهورة.
The fourth face: that it occurs due to the proponent's silence about a premise that is not well-known, out of fear that the opponent would deny it if he stated it explicitly. For example, someone who stipulates intention (niyya) in wudu and ghusl says: 'Everything that is an act of worship requires intention, like prayer.' He remains silent about the minor premise, which is: 'Wudu and ghusl are acts of worship.' The objector, like the Hanafi, says: 'I concede that every act of worship requires intention, but it does not follow from that that intention is required in wudu and ghusl.' If the proponent had stated the minor premise and the objector denied it, he would have left the realm of al-qawl bi'l-mujab. Some say in this example that the silence is about a well-known premise.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage explains the fourth face of al-qawl bi'l-mujab, which occurs when the proponent deliberately omits a premise (usually the minor premise in a syllogism) to avoid the objector directly denying it. The proponent states only the major premise and hopes the conclusion will be accepted. The objector responds by accepting the stated premise but denying that it entails the desired conclusion. The example concerns the requirement of intention (niyya) in ritual acts: the proponent argues that every act of worship requires intention (like prayer), but he omits the minor premise that wudu and ghusl are acts of worship. The objector (Hanafi) concedes the major premise but rejects the unstated conclusion, arguing that intention is not required for wudu and ghusl. If the proponent had stated the minor premise, the objector could have denied it, and then the response would no longer be al-qawl bi'l-mujab but direct objection. The author notes a difference of opinion: some scholars say that in this example, the omitted premise is actually well-known, not obscure.

Key Terms
سكوت عن مقدمة
مقدمة صغرى
مشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Because the major premise suffices to prove the conclusion
D) Because the opponent already conceded it

Key Takeaway
In the fourth face, the proponent's omission allows the objector to accept the stated premise yet challenge the conclusion, exploiting the missing premise.

Types of al-Qawl bi al-Mujib
Author defines and exemplifies the third and fourth types of al-qawl bi al-mujib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the third and fourth types of al-qawl bi al-mujib and apply them to analyze real debate examples from fiqh.

Core Idea
Al-qawl bi al-mujib accepts the premise but reveals an unstated flaw.

Al-qawl bi al-mujib is a key dialectical tool where the objector accepts the debater's premise but denies the conclusion by pointing out an implicit defect. In this lesson, we examine two specific types: when the debater's wording is too broad (third type) and when he omits a non-famous premise (fourth type). Through fiqh examples, you will learn to identify these moves and their weaknesses.

Guided Path
1
Identify the debater's argument
Find the debater's claim and his supporting premise(s). For example, the Hanafi's claim that zakat is due on horses because they are raced animals like camels.
2
Identify the objector's response
Note how the objector accepts the premise but qualifies or limits its application. In the third type, he says 'I accept it if' to restrict to an agreed case.
3
Determine the type of qawl bi al-mujib
Check if the objection arises from broad wording (third type) or from omitted premise (fourth type). Use the examples from the text (zakat of horses, intention in wudu/ghusl).
4
Assess the strength of the objection
The author notes the third type is weakest because the debater can clarify his intended meaning. Explain why.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هي قوله (قتل بما يقتل غالبًا لا ينافي القصاص)، فقوله (يجب فيه القصاص) ثبوت، ولأجل ما ورد فيه من الخلل عنده وقع عليه القول بالموجب المذكور.
الوجه الثالث : أن يقع لشمول لفظ المستدل صورة متفقا عليها، فيحمله المعترض على تلك الصورة، ويبقى النزاع فيما عداها . كقول الحنفي في وجوب زكاة الخيل حيوان يسابق عليه فتجب فيه الزكاة كالإبل . فيقول المعترض : أقول به إذا كانت الخيل للتجارة، وهذا أضعف أنواعه ؛ لأن المستدل يقول : عنيت وجوب الزكاة في رقابها .
الوجه الرابع : أن يقع لأجل سكوت المستدل عن مقدمة غير مشهورة؛ مخافة منع الخصم لها لو صرح بها . كقول مشترط النية في الوضوء والغُسل : كل ماهو قُربة تُشترط فيه النية كالصلاة ويسكت عن المقدمة الصغرى التي هي : الوضوء والغسل قُربة . فيقول المعترض كالحنفي : أُسلّم أن كل ماهو قربة تُشترط فيها النية ، ولا يلزم من ذلك اشتراطها في الوضوء والغُسل . فلو صرح المستدل بالصغرى فمنعها المعترض خرج عن القول بالموجب. وبعضهم يقول في هذا المثال السكوت عن مقدمة مشهورة.
ووجه الأول أن المشهورة كالمذكورة فكأنها غير مسكوت عنها لشهرتها، وهذا النوع من القول بالموجب إنما ورد على السكوت عنها . ووجه قول من قال ( عن مقدمة مشهورة) هو أن الشهرة هي سبب جواز الحذف ؛ لأن حذف غير المشهور قد يؤدي إلى عدم فهم الكلام .
واعلم بأن بعض أهل الأصول يقول : إن القول بالموجب والقلب كلاهما معارضة في الحكم. وقد ذكرنا أنه الصواب في القلب سابقا، وجعلهما الفخر الرازي من القوادح في العلة .
واظهرُ القولين أنه إن قال : هذا الذي نفيت ليس مبنى مذهبي) أنه يُصدّق في ذلك، فإن استنتج من الدليل ما يتوهم أنه محل النزاع أو ملازمه فقال المعترض : ليس) هو محل النزاع ولا ملازما له فجوابه هو محل النزاع أو ملازم له بطريق من طرق الإثبات .
والحق أن القول بالموجب يرد على جميع الأدلة، قياسًا كان الدليل أو غيره، فقول الرازي إنه من القوادح في العلة لا يظهر كلَّ الظهور ؛ لأنه أعم من ذلك .
وتطبيق القول بالموجب على الاعتراض في البحث والمناظرة أنه شبيه بالنقض ; لأن المعترض يقول : دليلك مسلم، ولكن الحكم الذي مدلوله متخلف عنه، وتخلف المدلول عن الدليل نقض في البحث والمناظرة، ولكنه عندهم مبطل للدليل؛ لأن تخلف المدلول عندهم هو النقض .
It is his statement: 'Killing by what kills generally does not preclude qisas'. So his statement 'It requires qisas' is established, and because of the defect that occurred in it according to him, the aforementioned qawl bi al-mujib occurred upon it. The third type: that it occurs due to the comprehensiveness of the debater's wording covering an agreed-upon case, so the objector carries it to that case, leaving the dispute for other cases. Like the Hanafi's statement regarding the obligation of zakat on horses: 'An animal raced upon, so zakat is obligatory on it, like camels.' So the objector says: 'I accept it if the horses are for trade.' And this is the weakest of its types because the debater says: 'I meant the obligation of zakat on their substance.' The fourth type: that it occurs due to the debater's silence about an unfamiliar premise, fearing the opponent would deny it if stated explicitly. Like the one who stipulates intention in wudu and ghusl saying: 'Every act of worship requires intention, like prayer,' and remains silent about the minor premise which is: 'Wudu and ghusl are acts of worship.' So the objector, like the Hanafi, says: 'I concede that every act of worship requires intention, but that does not entail that it is required in wudu and ghusl.' Had the debater stated the minor premise, the objector would have denied it, leaving the qawl bi al-mujib. And some say regarding this example that it is silence about a famous premise. The justification of the first view is that the famous is like the mentioned, as if it is not silent about due to its fame. And this type of qawl bi al-mujib is only applicable to silence about it. The justification of the one who said (about a famous premise) is that fame is the reason allowing omission, because omitting the unfamiliar might lead to misunderstanding. Know that some scholars of usul say: Qawl bi al-mujib and qalb are both objections in the ruling. We have previously mentioned that the correct opinion regarding qalb is that. Fakhr al-Razi considered both as defects in the cause. And the more apparent of the two opinions is that if he says: 'What you have denied is not the basis of my doctrine,' he is to be believed in that. If from the proof he deduces what is thought to be the point of dispute or its concomitant, and the objector says: 'It is not the point of dispute nor its concomitant,' then his answer is that it is the point of dispute or its concomitant by one of the methods of proof. The truth is that qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, whether the proof is a syllogism or otherwise. So al-Razi's statement that it is among the defects in the cause is not wholly apparent because it is more general than that. The application of qawl bi al-mujib to objection in investigation and debate is that it resembles naqd because the objector says: 'Your proof is accepted, but the ruling that is its implication is not present,' and the absence of the implication from the proof is naqd in investigation and debate, and according to them it invalidates the proof because the absence of the implication according to them is naqd.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the concept of 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' (accepting the premise but denying the conclusion) in dialectical debate. The author begins by recapping a specific example from fiqh: the statement 'Killing by what kills generally does not preclude qisas' – here the objector accepts the premise but points out a defect. Then the author outlines two types (wujuh) of qawl bi al-mujib: The third type occurs when the debater's wording is so broad that it covers an agreed-upon case; the objector interprets it as referring only to that case, leaving the dispute for other cases. The example given is from Hanafi jurisprudence: the claim that zakat is due on horses because they are raced animals, like camels. The objector says 'I accept it if the horses are for trade,' which is a weak type because the debater meant zakat on the substance (raqaba), not on trade value. The fourth type arises when the debater deliberately omits a non-famous premise for fear of denial. For instance, someone arguing that intention is required in wudu and ghusl says: 'Every act of worship requires intention, like prayer,' but keeps silent about the minor premise that wudu and ghusl are acts of worship. The objector (e.g., a Hanafi) concedes the major premise but denies the conclusion because the minor premise is not established. The author notes a dispute about whether the omitted premise is famous or not, and explains both views: the first view says that a famous premise is treated as if it were mentioned, so silence about it does not count for qawl bi al-mujib; the second view says that fame is precisely what allows omission, so silence about a famous premise still falls under qawl bi al-mujib. The author then addresses the relationship between qawl bi al-mujib and qalb (reversal). Some usul scholars say both are objections in the ruling, but the author had already clarified the correct view on qalb earlier. Fakhr al-Razi considered both as defects in the cause ('illa). The author favors the opinion that if the debater says 'What you denied is not the basis of my doctrine,' he is believed. Then the author notes the truth: qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, not just syllogisms, so al-Razi's restriction to defects in the cause is inadequate. Finally, the author likens qawl bi al-mujib to naqd (refutation) because in both cases the objector says 'Your proof is valid but its implication does not hold,' which invalidates the proof. However, in the jargon of debate, the absence of the implication is called naqd.

Key Terms
القول بالموجب
المقدمة المشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third type of al-qawl bi al-mujib, why does the objector accept the debater's premise?
A) Because the premise is false
B) Because the debater's wording is too broad and covers an agreed-upon case
C) Because the objector wants to change the subject
D) Because the premise is famous

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth types of al-qawl bi al-mujib reveal that accepting a premise can still lead to dispute if the premise is too broad or if a crucial premise is omitted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The objector says: 'I accept that every intoxicant is forbidden, but I do not accept that nabidh is an intoxicant.' Is this an example of al-qawl bi al-mujib? If so, which type? Explain.

Al-Qawl bi al-Mujib, Qalb, and Naqd
Author compares qawl bi al-mujib with qalb and naqd, and critiques al-Razi's limitation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compare al-qawl bi al-mujib with qalb and naqd, and evaluate the scope of al-qawl bi al-mujib as argued by the author against al-Razi.

Core Idea
Al-qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, not just defects in the cause.

Beyond the specific types, the author discusses how al-qawl bi al-mujib relates to other dialectical concepts like qalb (reversal) and naqd (refutation). He critiques Fakhr al-Razi's view that it is only a defect in the cause, arguing it is more general. This lesson explores these relationships and the author's reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Compare qawl bi al-mujib and qalb
Note that some usul scholars consider both as objections in the ruling. The author had earlier clarified qalb.
2
Examine al-Razi's classification
Al-Razi considered both as defects in the cause. Identify his reasoning from the passage.
3
Understand the author's critique
The author says al-Razi's view is not wholly apparent because qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, not just syllogisms.
4
Link to naqd
The author likens qawl bi al-mujib to naqd because both involve accepting the proof but denying the implication. In debate terminology, absence of implication is naqd.

The truth is that qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, whether the proof is a syllogism or otherwise. So al-Razi's statement that it is among the defects in the cause is not wholly apparent because it is more general than that. The application of qawl bi al-mujib to objection in investigation and debate is that it resembles naqd because the objector says: 'Your proof is accepted, but the ruling that is its implication is not present,' and the absence of the implication from the proof is naqd in investigation and debate, and according to them it invalidates the proof because the absence of the implication according to them is naqd.
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is an extended commentary on the concept of 'al-qawl bi al-mujib' (accepting the premise but denying the conclusion) in dialectical debate. The author begins by recapping a specific example from fiqh: the statement 'Killing by what kills generally does not preclude qisas' – here the objector accepts the premise but points out a defect. Then the author outlines two types (wujuh) of qawl bi al-mujib: The third type occurs when the debater's wording is so broad that it covers an agreed-upon case; the objector interprets it as referring only to that case, leaving the dispute for other cases. The example given is from Hanafi jurisprudence: the claim that zakat is due on horses because they are raced animals, like camels. The objector says 'I accept it if the horses are for trade,' which is a weak type because the debater meant zakat on the substance (raqaba), not on trade value. The fourth type arises when the debater deliberately omits a non-famous premise for fear of denial. For instance, someone arguing that intention is required in wudu and ghusl says: 'Every act of worship requires intention, like prayer,' but keeps silent about the minor premise that wudu and ghusl are acts of worship. The objector (e.g., a Hanafi) concedes the major premise but denies the conclusion because the minor premise is not established. The author notes a dispute about whether the omitted premise is famous or not, and explains both views: the first view says that a famous premise is treated as if it were mentioned, so silence about it does not count for qawl bi al-mujib; the second view says that fame is precisely what allows omission, so silence about a famous premise still falls under qawl bi al-mujib. The author then addresses the relationship between qawl bi al-mujib and qalb (reversal). Some usul scholars say both are objections in the ruling, but the author had already clarified the correct view on qalb earlier. Fakhr al-Razi considered both as defects in the cause ('illa). The author favors the opinion that if the debater says 'What you denied is not the basis of my doctrine,' he is believed. Then the author notes the truth: qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs, not just syllogisms, so al-Razi's restriction to defects in the cause is inadequate. Finally, the author likens qawl bi al-mujib to naqd (refutation) because in both cases the objector says 'Your proof is valid but its implication does not hold,' which invalidates the proof. However, in the jargon of debate, the absence of the implication is called naqd.

Key Terms
القول بالموجب
القلب
النقض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with al-Razi's view that qawl bi al-mujib is a defect in the cause?
A) It is completely wrong
B) It is too narrow because qawl bi al-mujib applies to all proofs
C) It is too broad because qawl bi al-mujib only applies to syllogisms
D) It confuses qawl bi al-mujib with qalb

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi al-mujib is a versatile dialectical tool that is not confined to syllogistic defects; it can be used in any argument where the premise is accepted but the conclusion is contested.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a debate where a Maliki argues that 'every prayer is invalidated by laughing' and the objector says: 'I accept that, but your prayer was not invalidated because you did not laugh.' Is this qawl bi al-mujib, qalb, or naqd? Explain using the author's distinctions.
